adjourned until 10 o'clock Thursday
I morning.
About an hour and a half before the
State Commission adjourned the local
board got the notion of holding a meet-
Ing of its own. A room was quickly
j selected and in a few minutes a troop
of witnesses, mostly employes of the
i asylum, was in attendance. Prominent
among them were T. T. Tourtillott and
Mrs. Madigan, the two accused of un
becoming conduct by Charles L. Wil
liams. There were present also Dr.
Sponogle and Miss Louise Bambauer,
acting matron by grace of Dr. Spo
nogle and the will of Director Gould.
Dr. Cnrnow occupied the chair. Di
rector White was before the State
Commission and Directors Upham,
Hale and Gould began the investiga
tion.
Mr?. Madigan came in pale, self-con
scious and exceedingly nervous. It
w.-i.s some time before she ceased biting
her lips and twitching them in a vain
effort to appear unmoved. Tourtlllott
bore the ordeal with somewhat less
embarrassment.
Chairman Curnow announced the |
purpose of the meeting to be the in- j
vestlgation of the charges filed by
Charles 1,. Williams against T. T. |
Tourtlllott and Mrs. Madiean. After j
the secretary rend them, as they have I
been published in The Call, Hale asked
for an expression from the board as to
whether Gould was to be considered
competent to sit In the case, his I
brother, Tourtlllott, being one of
the persona accused. It was fin
ally decided to allow Gould to partici
pate in the proceedings, but not to
v t. on any question or take part In
the final decision. Hale then moved
to proceed with the investigation, and
the motion was carried.
Mrs. Olivia B. Williams was the first
witness. She related her story in a
clear manner. The facts she pave were
the basis of the charges of Immoral
conduct made against Mrs. Kate Madi
gan and T. T. Tourtillott. She said
thai about the middle of last October.
somewhere near 5:30 o'clock in the
morning, when she opened the door
leading into ward F in the female part
of the hospital she heard the latch of
Mrs. Madigan's door click. A few
minutes later Mrs. Madigan came out,
looked around, and again retired to her i
room. The witness walked to the end
of the corridor and then down the
stairway, at the foot of which she |
waited. A few minutes later she saw i
Tourtillott coming toward her and she j
met him face to face. He passed with- |
out speaking to her. Mrs. Williams re- i
ported the matter to Dr. Christal. A i
few days later and about the same j
time wrote to Gould and told him of
Tourtillott's actions In the women's
ward, and asked him to speak to his j
brother. The witness also related th<Â» :
circumstance to her husband and
Nighlwatehman Kerns.
Dr. Christal reported the matter to
Dr. Sponogle and the medical director
summoned Mrs. Williams to his office
and questioned her about It. He cau
tioned her that unless she told the
truth she could not stay In the lnstitu
tion two minutes. Shortly afterward j
Mrs. Williams saw Gould at the asy
lum. Gould called her into the office
and asked her why she told Dr. Chris
tal of the affair when she had written j
to him (Gould). He said he did not
believe her statement, and made
threats that If she did not keep quiet
h^ would give her a long vacation.
Gould tried to shake Mrs. Williams'
testimony by a number of questions
and asked if he hnd not said that Dr.
Sponogle and he had arrived at the
conclusion lhat there was no truth In
the statement. At this time Gould was
called before the State Lunacy Com
mission and White took his place in
the board.
Mrs. Williams explained the position
of Tourtillott, Mrs. Madigan and her
self on the morning in question. She
had told Dr. Christal of Tourtillott be
ing in the female ward because Dr.
Christal waa In charge of thnt ward.
Hale then took a hand in the ques
tioning. He asked when Mrs. Williams
was married nnd wanted to see hÂ»r !
marriatre certiliente; also who per- i
formed the ceremony and whether she j
had seen the marriage license. Will- j
iama answered this question for his i
wife. He said they were married in j
San Francisco on September 8, 1897, by
a Justice of the Peace. The license was
procured from "Cupid" Danforth, and
was a matter of record in San Fran
cisco County.
Mrs. Williams said the lights were
out in the corridor at the time she dis
cerned Tourtillott and Mrs. Madigan.
Hale doubted whether it was light
enough to see distinctly at f>:3o o'clock
in the morning in the middle of Octo
ber. The calendar was consulted and !
it showed that on October 1R the sun
rose at 6:18 o'clock.
About two months after her mar
riage Mrs. Williams informed Dr.
Christal and Dr. Sponogle of the fact.
She said they had gone up to San Fran
cisco on her day off, got married and
returned the next day.
At this point Williams, who was art-
Ing a.s counsel for his wife, attempted
to question her, but Hale gagged him.
He held that during the absence of
Gould, who represented Tourtillott,
Williams had no right to question the
witness. White said Williams had as
much right as Gould to question the
witness, as he was her husband and
counsel. Williams was not granted the
privilege.
Mrs. Williams further stated that one
night Mrs. Madigan's light was burning
at 12 o'clock, and Night "Watchman
Kerns told Miss Fogarty to turn out
the light. At 5 o'clock the next morn-
ing Mrs. Madlgan returned to her
room. She often went to her room at
that hour. About the month of Janu
ary Mrs. Madigan confided to Mrs. Wil
liams that she and Tourtlllott were
married. This was in explanation of
their conduct, and Mrs. Madigan asked
Mrs. Williams not to mention it to any
one.
Mrs. Z. B. White had been employed
at the asylum for two years. She had
never seen Tourtillott in the woman's
ward near Mrs. Madigan's room. When
Mrs. Williams was discharged she told
the witness that her knowledge of the
acts of Mrs. Madigan and Tourtillott
had been the cause of her dismissal.
The witness never saw anything about
Mrs. Madigan or Tourtillott that was
improper. She stated positively that
j she knew nothing of the matter. She
had no recollection of having remarked
to Mrs. Williams that the Madigan-
Tourtillott scandal was common talk
about the building. Miss Rose Ryan,
an attendant, knew nothing of the af
fair before the board. She denied
ever having been sent by Miss Royse
to Tourtillotfs rocim to tell Mrs. Madi
gan to go to her own room.
Miss Rose Ryan further testi
fied that she knew nothing about either
Tourtillott or Mrs. Madigan that was
not to their credit.
Dr. Sponogle testified that his recol
lection of the story Mrs. Williams told
him In October did not agree with that
told to the board. She had told him
that she thought it was Mr. Tourtillott
whom she had seen, but was not posi
tive.
"I also asked her," continued the wit
ness, "if she had seen him at any other
time at the came place, and she ans
wered that she had not."
To a question of Director Upham the
witness replied that he had not heard
of a report from any other person, and
added that the accused always had the
reputation of being a lady and a gen
tleman, respectively, in every regard.
In response to Director Hale, Dr.
Sponogle said that after he had been
told of the affair by Mrs. Williams, Mr.
Hale had given him instructions to
have the nlghtwatches keep a sharp
lookout for a repetition of the occur
rence. He had done so. but nothing
further had been reported to him. He
had then investigated the matter close
ly, but had been unable to obtain any
evidence from any other source.
Director Hale corroborated the latter
part of Dr. Sponogle's testimony. Un
der cross-examination by Mr. Williams,
who was allowed to appear as a cour
tesy, Dr. Sponogle stated he did not
remember that Mrs. Williams had told
him she had informed Nightwatchman
Kerns of the early morning meeting
with Tourtillott.
"Did she ever tell you," continued
Williams, "that she had seen Mr. Tour
tillott and Mrs. Madigan together in
the laundry? I think before we get
through we will be able to show that
this is a fact."
The witness replied in the negative.
"Didn't she say that she did not want
it to go any further, as she did not
want to make any trouble for Mr.
Gould, who was a friend of hers?"
asked Williams.
To this Dr. Sponogle made an evasive
reply, which caused Williams to re
mark:
"It seems that this Institution has
been terrified to such an extent that
every one lias to guard his tongue, nnd
the truth is not so important to them
as their bread and butter."
Dr. Sponogle further testified that he
had reported the matter to the chair
man of the Management Committee,
Mr. Could, but had not reported it to
any other member of the board.
Williams wn.s frequently interrupted
In his cross-examination by Gould's
and Hale's objections, which were in
variably sustained by Chairman Cur
now, notwithstanding that whenever
Williams offered any objections to mat
ter clearly irrevelant he was overruled
by the Chairman. Gould brought out
the opinion from Dr. Sponogle that the
work of Tourtillott had always been
satisfactory and that his conduct had
been the same. Witness had never
heard the moral character of Tourtil
lott questioned before this accusation
was made and he added that he did not
believe the statement made by Mrs.
Williams.
"State to the board," requested Mr.
Gould, "what the mental condition of
Mrs. Williams was during the latter
months of her employment here."
This brought an objection from Wil
liams, who informed the board that he
had recommendations from the offi
cials of the institution as to her com
petency, moral character, intelligence
and truthfulness. He was not allowed
to offer them in evidence, though
Chairman Curnow admitted he had
given Mrs. Williams such recommenda
tion. Williams added that if his wife's
sanity was in question at any time dur
ing her employment it was clearly the
duty of the board to have discharged
her.
At this point Gould gave the cue to |
the chairman to apply the gag to Wil- j
Hams, and it was duly and promptly
done. Dr. Sponogle continued:
"I think she acted peculiarly at times.
I think she passed eight months or a
little longer in this way. She im
agined she had become a great news
paper writer, and at other times she
thought she had become quite a singer.
She also tried to become a great pen
man, and instead of retiring in
the morning when her duties were
over she would practice penmanship.
From observing these peculiarities I
thought it was undesirable ior her to
remain in this institution. I did not
think that with her family history she
would be benefited in this institution.
Her mother had died in an asylum, but
of her father I know nothing."
This was all brought out by the
questioning of Gould.
Williams asked the witness If he had
not promised to give Mrs. Williams a
recommendation, and if he had not
stated that* she was conpetent. faith
ful and truthful. Dr. Sponogle made
no answer.
F. P. Kerns, the ni^ht watchman,
testified that he had never seen any
thing blameworthy in the conduct of
either Mr. Tourtillott or Mrs. Madlgan.
Dr. J. F. Christal testified that Mrs.
Williams had told him of the Tour
tillott-Mr.d'^an 1Â» Ident two Âays after
it had occurred, and that he had re
ported it to Dr. Sponogle.
Tourtillott and Mrs. Madigan were
called to the stand and made general
denials of all the charges.
Mrs. E. Templeton, Mrs. Watkins. H.
A. Braden and Miss Julia Creighton,
all employes of 'he asylum, testified to
the good character and reputation of i
the accused, and also that of Mrs.
Williams. They knew nothing of the
alleged misdoings of the accused.
Detective Erwln Frost said Williams
had come to him to get his services in |
the case. Williams wanted to force the [
managers to reinstate his wife, so that :
he could go East and study law. Frost i
said Williams put him on the track of !
certain things against Dr. Sponogle,
which, if true, ought to cause the doc
tor to leave the State. Williams had no
money to pay the witness and agreed
to liquidate the Indebtedness as soon
as Mrs. Williams got at work again.
It was during Frost's examination
that Williams and his wife left the
room and departed for home.
Chairman Curnow declared the case
closed, and intimated that he desired
the board to go at once into executive
session to decide the matter, though
Williams had asked previously for the
opportunity to produce additional wit
nesses whom he had not been able to
reach, owing to the extremely short
time he had been given to notify them.
Gould pleaded to have another witness
examined, and his wish was not rain
said.
F. A. McDonald, an employe, was
called He stated that Mrs. Williams
had told him that she was sorr- that
she had gone into the matter, but that
she had been urged to do so by her hus
band.
Last of all, when nearly every one
had left the room, came the attack on
Mrs. Williams' reputation. It was made
by Miss Louise Bambauer. She stated
that Mrs. Williams had confided to her
that she was infatuated with Mr. Tour
tillott and that she had desired the
witness to bring about a meeting be
tween herself and Tourtillott. She
added that she thought Mrs. Williams
had brought about this "disturbance"
in the hope that Mrs. Madigan would
be sent away and "she would then have
absolute control." Miss Bambauer con
cluded her testimony and the farce of
bearing witness to the good character
of the accused.
Gould moved for an executive ses
sion, but his motion received no second.
On motion of Hale, an adjournment
was taken until Thursday morning at
10 o'clock.

WHERE MILES LANDED.
ZZJ VER since the beginning of the war The Call
has made an intelligent study of the situation.
* It has not only printed accurate news from the
front, but has endeavored with more than gratifying
success to foreshadow the outcome of army move
ments as indicated by the logic of events. Thus it
announced where, in its belief, General Miles would
land in Porto Rico, and had enough confidence in
its judgment to publish a picture of the place. The
picture was also accurate.
Circumstances sustained the conjecture. General
Miles had not announced his plans in advance. The
War Department had guarded them carefully, and
yet an analysis of the situation made plain that there
was only one course for him to pursue. The Call
merely assumed that he would follow this course, in
stead of attempting another, which could but result
in failure. It is not the habit of this paper to venture
haphazard guesses.
RESPECT FOR THE FLfIG.
ALL over the city the national colors have been
flying for months. The flag floats above pub
lic buildings, from lofty staffs without number
and in front of many residences it has been unfurled.
All this is gratifying. It shows an American spirit
aroused to expression by the crisis through which
the country is passing. But there is still something
lacking.
Many of the flags put in place weeks ago have
received no attention. Buffeted and tugged at by
the breezes during the day; they have been left out
at night, exposed to the fogs and mists. As a re
sult they have been soiled, and in instances not only
whipped to tatters, but the remains do not present to
the eye the inspiriting red, white and blue. On the
contrary, the colors have merged to a draggled
combination hardly suggesting the flag. This strikes
the observer as neglect.
There is no particular reason why the flag should
fly after night. Let it be on view during the day.
Let it catch the glint of the morning sun and throw
its shadow eastward when the sunset gun booms
at the Presidio. Then it should be taken down. To
see it flaunting torn, bedraggled and forgotten is
worse than not seeing it. The impression created
is that enthusiasm has passed, and that the impulse
which caused the flag to be run up was momentary.
Common sentiment protects the flag against dese
cration. It is forbidden that one should deface it.
And in exposing it to the elements to be shredded,
stained, raveled, to become only a hint of what it
was when first displayed, is a form of desecration.
NOT ASKING PERMISSION.
AGAIN there comes the report that the powers
of Europe will not permit the United States
to keep the Philippines. So far as known, this
country has not asked their permission. It does not
care what they desire, and will go ahead on the lines
which seem wisest, regardless of Kaiser, Czar or any
other variety of potentate.
There is a strong party in this country opposed to
the taking of the Philippines. They object to it as a
matter of policy. They think it contrary to the
spirit of the constitution. But even these do not
weigh the opinions of the "powers." They think, in
deed, that in attending to affairs which concern them
selves the powers will be amply employed. In com
mon with the rest of America, they resent the im
pertinence of interference. Perhaps the United
States will not keep the Philippines. It may be the
conservative element will prove dominant, and that
the impulse toward territorial expansion will never
get further than a vague and unformed ambition.
But there is one way in which Europe can force
the United States to keep the islands, and this is to
emphasize the dictum that the United States must
give them up. We are not looking for trouble.
There is no chip on our shoulder. The nation prays
for honorable peace. But there is not in all Europe
a nation big enough, nor a combination big enough,
to tell us what we shall do. This is our fight, un
sought It is American blood that Is being spent;
American money pays the cost of campaign. We
want no advice, and we will not tolerate orders.
Naturally enough the Spanish hare Deen relat
ing some of the rules of surrender. If there are
any rules they do not know about they have only to
be told of them and they will with the same cheerful
celerity violate these also.
John Sherman should not submit to interviews, and
his friends ought to guard him against the tempta
tion. There was a time when his opinion was of
value.
Cubans are now afraid of American annexation,
but if they were not scared about this they would be
torn by fears of something else.
The war has developed the singular and pleasing
fact that every regiment is a crack organization.
THfIT WOODLAND FELLOW.
THE editor of the Woodland Mall, who volun
teered to serve his country in a military ca
pacity, with a string to his patriotism, has been
left unnoticed, until he should fill out the catalogue
of his deficiencies in decency and good character.
In a recent issue of his paper he seems to have
finished the list, and we pay him now final attention.
He publishes a telegram sent to him from Carson
City, Nevada, dated July 23, as follows:
"Sam Leake of The Call asked his Carson cor
respondent to find out the names of the four Wood
land men who refused to muster in."
The editor thereupon says: "This demonstrates
the malignant spirit of this man Leake and the Budd
organ he is running. He seems bent on persecut
ing the Woodland men who defied his boss, Jim
Budd."
Now let this fellow take a look at nimself in the
mirror of the facts. On July 22 it was wired from
Carson that "six members of Company A failed to
muster in to-day, having skipped out. All of them
came from California originally."
Anxious to save the credit of California by spot
ting these six skulkers, and by getting their names
to make the responsibility personal and lift it from
the State, The Call wired its correspondent at Carson
this dispatch, see files of Western Union office :
"Davis, Carson, Nevada: Send particulars failure
members Company A to muster in, giving names and
places they came from. CALL."
This was filed at 12:30 a. m., the 23d. It made no
mention of Woodland or any place, even of the
State, and by using the word "places" proved that
no one place was in mind. The answer published
In our issue of the 23d gave the names of the men,
said they were hoboes, picked off brakebeams at
Reno and Winnemucca, who enlisted to get a few
square meals, and giving their residence as California.
This relieved the State of responsibility for the va
garies of a lot of tramps, and the incident closed, un
til it was reopened by the editor of the Mail, who
persists in showing a fellow feeling for skulkers by
taking up their cause and abusing all who rebuke
them.
To go back to his grievance. He was an enlisted
man in the Woodland company. The Governor of
California, by virtue of his legal authority, appointed
the officers of the company. This was seized as a
pretext by the editorial warrior to escape from the
service with a whole skin, which he seems to value
more highly than he does his country. Industrious
cultivation of discontent gained recruits to his pur
pose, and many members of the company refused to
be sworn into the Federal service, and were after
ward mustered out of the State militia. The Gov
ernor is the commander-in-chief of the National
Guard. He acted within hii plain grant of author
ity, and whether his act was distasteful or not to the
men, their oath required their obedience to it. If
the commander-in-chief had withdrawn his action
at their dictation, discipline would have been at an
end. He did just what the President has been called
on to do nearly every day since war was declared.
Not every private in the ranks is expected to be
pleased with the brigade and division officers desig
nated by the President, but a soldier's first duty is
obedience and he has to accept the officers ap
pointed over him by the final authority. Perhaps
no appointment was ever more strongly opposed than
that of Brigadier General Harrison Gray Otis. The
most bitter criticism of it came from California. But
the President made it, and The Call, which had given
the opposition a chance to be heard in Its columns,
at once set the example of good citizenship by cheer
ful acquiescence in the act of the final authority. The
editor of the Woodland Mail, under similar circum
stances, showed that in his makeup there is not a
single fiber of good citizenship, nor in all his blood
a drop that is reddened by patriotism. It is evident
that he enlisted in a spirit of vainglory, for the tem
porary titillation of the flattery of his neighbors, and
retreated on the first opportunity to back out of a
promise he had not the courage to keep. With
him. no doubt, went several gallant men whose sense
of duty was for the moment obscured by the griev
ance he invented and propagated. They were placed
in a painful position they will long rrgret, and which
their friends will gladly forget, if this scribbling
coward, who had not the courage to swap his scissors
for a sword, will permit the incident to pass into ob
livion. But the incorrigible poltroon seems deter-
mined to glorify his skulk into a virtue, by lying
: about The Call. If he be a patriot, if his enlist
i ment were in good faith, he has had opportunity to
1 p r ove it by re -enlisting in any other company. But,
â  omitting this proof, the world is warranted in the
: conclusion that the army is stronger for his absence
from the ranks and that civil life is degraded by his
return to it. His neighbors will long remember
when Dungan, the dunghill, went briefly into busi
ness as a gamecock, but soon skulked back to scratch
and crow in the same old place.
INDUSTRIAL DEMOCRACY,
f~\ T the present time we have among the political
/ \ factions of the United States a Conservative
* Democracy, a Bryanite Democracy, a Fusion
Democracy, a Populist Democracy and a Social
Democracy, not counting the Coxey Democracy
which still lingers in Ohio to some extent. Now
we are to have another Democracy, if certain Los
Angeles men have their way, for in that city there
has been started a movement to form an "Industrial
Democracy."
Under normal conditions an organization calling
itself "industrial" would be devoted to the pursuits
of industry. If it did not undertake some vast work
like the Nicaragua canal it would at least help its
votaries to get a job at fruit picking. At all haz
ards it would keep out of politics, for nothing is so
destructive to genuine industry as the political habit.
It might, therefore, be supposed that the proposed
Industrial Democracy is to be an association to pro
mote the industries of the land on the Democratic
plan of making every man his own boss, but that
supposition would be erroneous.
We do not live under normal conditions, or at
least Democracy is no longer normal. Coxey's army,
it will be remembered, called itself an "industrial
army," though its campaign began and ended in a
tramp, and its force dispersed when ordered by a
policeman in Washington to keep off the grass.
Therefore it will surprise none to learn that the pro
posed Industrial Democracy of Los Angeles is to
be, when organized, a political faction, and cherishes
the hope of eventually becoming a political party
with a candidate for the Presidency and every local
office.
The platform of the organization is one of the
kind designed to sweep everything of a public na
ture, except the streets. It calls for the public
ownership of all monopolies, including land and
trusts. Now, imagine a government owning a trust
and trying to make it work industrially. Next it
calls for entire civil service reform and "the ap
pointment of all officials on their merits for the par
ticular office in which there is a vacancy." Direct
legislation, the initiative and the referendum, propor
tional representation and the imperative mandate fol
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, JULY 27, 1898,
low, as a matter of course. Every new party in
these days has to have those things, or it won't be
allowed to play.
Up to that point there is nothing in the platform
of principles that in the slightest degree refers to
work or industry. Just here, however, the framers
of the instrument seem to have had a second thought,
for in the typewritten copy of the circular sent to us
by the organizers there is at this point inserted with
pen and ink a fourth plank, demanding "immediate
employment of the unemployed upon public work."
For the rest the circular contents itself with an ap
peal to the people for support on the ground that
the time has come "for an honest declaratiop of is
sues that will deliver us from bondage to monopoly,
and from industrial and political practices that are
destroying the higher American life."
We have no desire to comment on the scheme as
a political movement. The more kinds of Democ
racy there are the merrier life is, and the less impor
tant is it to fight any of them, since they fight them
selves with such a Donnybrook gayety. But as an
industrial proposition we cannot forbear four words
of reproof t the thing won't work.
AN incontrovertible evidence of Judge Maguire's
political insincerity is furnished by his willing
ness to desert his post in Congress for a quest
of the Governorship of California. This he is bound
to do in the coming campaign, unless the Democrats
can discover some plan by which they can run him
for Governor and Congress at the same time. We
know of no instance in which this has been done, but
nothing is impossible to Democrats and Populists.
According to the Democratic leaders the great is
sue of the campaign will be the railroad funding
amendment, worked through Congress shortly be
fore adjournment by the Ã¼biquitous Huntington.
Now that that amendment is law we do not exactly
see how the Democrats are going to defeat it, but
this also may be one of the things which are not
impossible to the fusionists. To a rational man it
would seem that the only thing now to be done is
to await the action of the Funding Commission and
disagree with it
At all events, whatever the result, Judge Maguire
cannot, as Governor of California, defeat Hunting
ton. If, as his admirers fay, he is the bete noir
of the railroad magnate, his place is in Congress,
where he can occasionally meet and give the old
man a battle. Huntington visits this State once a
year, and then only for the purpose of looking over
his property. Unless Judge Maguire calls at the
yellow building during office hours and sends in his
card, he will never even see the millionaire, much
less be presented with an opportunity of meeting him
in forensic contest.
The truth of the matter Is, Judge Maguire, like
all demagogues, is ready at any moment to abandon
important work anywhere for a fat office. He has
no stomach for fighting Huntington-s Central Pa
cific funding schemes when there is a desirable office
In prospect. His predominant instinct is place
hunting, and no sacrifice of principle or neglect of
duty is too great to appall him. If Maguire were
a man of steadfast purpose, devoted to the mainte
nance of the principles upon which he has founded
all the reputation he possesses, he would stay in Con
gress and fight Huntington, Instead of seeking an
office in which his chief duty will consist of appoint
ing notaries and jobbing in patronage. His anxious
desire to make a change at this time is proof positive
that he knows no duty except the duty of at all
times getting, if possible, into the best office in
sight.
SPAIN'S DESIRE FOR PEACE.
\A ADRID dispatches announce that immense
/ \ importance is attached there to the recent
* * statement of Secretary Day favoring peace
on terms that would enable the Spanish Government
to avoid the necessity of continuing a war of despair.
Spain, it is said, is keenly eager to find an opportun
ity for opening peace negotiations, and will gladly
profit by the first that occurs. The recent repre
sentation made to the President by the French Min
ister confirms the report, and proves that Spain is
really eager for peace.
As a matter of fact, what Spain needs at this time
is some official who will tell her plainly it is time
to quit. It will be remembered that when the Maine
was blown up in Havana harbor, the â¢seaman on
watch, a certain Bill Anthony, as soon as the shock
was over, walked quietly up to the officer in charge
and with the formal naval salute said: "Excuse
me, sir; I have to report that the ship is blown up
and is sinking."
Doubtless the officer in charge was well aware of
the fact from personal observation, but it was grati
fying to him to have it announced in the regular
way, so that he could act upon it officially without
disarranging the etiquette of the navy. What Spain
lequires at this juncture is some straightforward Bill
Anthony, who will report in due form to the Queen
Regent and her Prime Minister that the nation is
blown up and is sinking. With such a report be
fore them, they would be able to deal wisely with
the situation without an infraction of the high re
quirements of Spanish honor and punctilio.
A proposition coming from our Government for
the opening of negotiations looking toward the re
storation of peace would be absurd. We have every
thing to gain by pushing the war vigorously, and
nothing to expect from an armistice. In all our
dealings with the Spanish Government in reference
to Cuban affairs, the Madrid officials showed them
selves past masters in the art of diplomatic evasion,
delay and tergiversation. They could give us cards
and spades and beat us at the game. It was not
until the ultimatum of war was given that we at
tained a superiority over them.
With that experience fresh In our minds we can
hardly be expected to agree to an armistice for fur^
ther negotiations. When Spain sues for peace in
the proper manner it will be forthcoming. In the
meantime the Spanish Government should take note
that the nation is sinking, and that if it desires to
save anything from the wreck it must act at once.
That Miss Schley should have been sent out of
Spain is altogether proper. She went there to see
the Queen Regent and discuss affairs of state. That
she was quietly requested to make herself scarce, in
stead of being conducted to the Lorder and ordered
to skip, is the first indication there has been of an
effort on the part of the Spanish to be polite.
Colorado is another State which resents the official
report that it was lax in offering the number of vol
unteers asked of it. As a matter of fact, the only
trouble with Colorado was that many more men of
fered their services than there was a place for.
It is to be hoped that Miles will waste no time
in splicing cables so as to talk with Washington.
The war in the West Indies has been marked by a
surplus of conversation.
Camara is to take refuge at Ceuta. It is fortunate
there is something a Spanish admiral can take.
MAGUIRE'S INSINCERITY.
THE CARPENTER MYSTERY-EXIT LOCHNER,
The second act of the little police comedy based upon the tragic murder
of Mrs. Sadie Carpenter was performed at the Coroner's Court yesterday,
and the result was, as everybody anticipated, the triumphant acQuittal of
Joseph Lochner. That is to say, Lochner was acquitted as far as it is pos
sible for a man who has never been charged with an offense. Though in
custody, he was legally free, yet practically, owing to the devious pol
methods of administration, he was on his trial before a Jury for the crime
of murder.
The man, who has endured his anomalous position with wonderful pa
tience, realized this himself. He actually took the trouble to address
Jury in his own defense, a proceeding which was entirely unnecessary,
considering the complete ruin into which the police case against hw
fallen. He pointed out, with logical clearness, that he had absolutely no
motive for committing such a crime; he had never even had a quarrel
with Mrs. Carpenter, but had always lived on the best of terms with hei
Further, if robbery had been his motive, where were the missing watch
and the money supposed to have been in the murdered woman's purse?
They were certainly not on his person when arrested, and he had had no
time to dispose of these incriminating articles.
The fact that the night clerk did not notice Loohner come home that
evening does not count for much, because night clerks in an establish
ment of this kind are frequently busy away from their offices. Indeed, the
evidence showed that this particular clerk used often to make up the beds
at night. Therefore, there is nothing to controvert Lochner's statement
that he came home late and went straight to bed. In support of It we have
evidence showing that at the time of the murder he was undressed. At
first the police claimed that Lochner could not have had time to dress be
tween his awakening by the night clerk and the arrival of the police. But,
on the other hand, we have the evidence of two men in the saloon opposite,
to whom Lochner announced the murder, wearing a nightgown, over which
hia clothes had been hastily thrown. It does not take an active man
long to throw on a pair of trousers and a coat, and we may reasonably as
sume that Lochner had ample time in which to hurriedly dress himself.
This clothing question deserves much more attention than the police
have placed upon it. Mrs. Riley, whooccupied the room next to Sadie Car
penter's, states positively that twenty minutes after the scuffle was over
she heard the door of the murdered woman's room slammed and footsteps
as of a person wearing creaking boots proceed down the passage. How far
the steps went she could not be sure, but as the hallway turns at a right
angle at the corner of Lochner's room, she could only have heard them for
a short distance, whether the man went Into that room or took his way
down the stairs.
Now, if this was Lochner, he must have been fully dressed, for It is ab
surd to Imagine a man putting on a pair of creaking boots and nothing else
in order to slip from one room to another, over a few yards of hallway.
But the point need not be discussed further. I have definitely settled the
fact that Lochner did not wear creaking boots on the night in question.
Boots that creak must be new and in good condition; hence the popular
saying that squeaking shoes have not been paid for.
Talking to Lochner in the courtroom yesterday, I asked him, casually,
â¢what kind of boots he wore that night. "The pair I have on now," he re
plied readily, "I have no others." I looked at the shoes. They were very
old and very shabby, and had but the merest pretense of worn-out soles.
Anything less likely to creak could hardly be imagined.
This settles the question. It was not Lochner who slammed the door and
creaked his way along the passage. If it was another man he must have
either made his way down the stairs, or else have concealed himself some
where in the house. In either case it was strange that the night clerk did
not see any one, for we are told by Mrs. Rlley that she found him in his
office shortly after the event. Could a man, carrying a large bundle, have
passed unobserved and made his way into the Btreet? Mrs. Anderson, the
mother of the murdered woman, made a very significant statement yester
day, though it did not appear In evidence. She asserts positively that most
of her daughter's best dresses are missing, aa well as her watch, and other
minor articles. But most Important of all, a very gorgeous silk bedspread
Is gone. Mrs. Anderson is absolutely sure her daughter would never have
parted with this elaborate lace trimmed article, because once she refused
an offer of forty dollars for it. All these things, if done up together, would
have made a very big parcel, a parcel so large that the improbability of
any one carrying it unobserved out of the house at such an hour in the
morning is very great.
This bedspread, conspicuous and easily recognizable, furnishes us with a
most valuable clew, and the man who finds it will have advanced far to
ward solving the mystery. If robbery was the only motive, he must have
been a daring thief who, after murdering a woman, ventured to carry such
a noticeable bundle away with him. If the things were not removed from
the house, they ought to have been found long ago. Had the police acted
promptly, and taken reasonable methods at the outset, much might have
been discovered ere this.
Of course It is easy to reason after the event, but that is no excuse for
blunders. Let us Imagine the course a skilled detective would have pur
sued if called on to follow up such a case. He would certainly have asso
ciated Lochner with the crime, but then he would have done it in a scienti
fic way. He would have treated the man purely as a witness, examined
him closely, but would never have allowed him to suppose for a moment
that he was suspected of complicity. Instead of shutting him up in prison
where he could furnish no possible clew, he would have left him at liberty'
under close and skillful espionage. In short, he would have given Lochner
plenty of rope, never letting him know that he was watched. If the man
had been guilty of the offense, he would have been sure, sooner or later to
betray himself; he would have been compelled to take some steps to disDose
of the stolen articles.
The loss of the clothing, and especially the bedspread, should have been
ascertair i long ago, and the whole house shoulu have been cloÂ«, P ly
searched tor the things. If not within the building, then every pawn shin
and second-hand dealer's store should have boen ransacked- the detectives
should have known no rest until they discovered the stolen property
Probably it is now too late to follow up this lin of investigation >r
one will douot that Captain Less has bungled, and worst of all he h
bungled in an illegal manner, openly defying the constitution ThY ,h a r
is, unless abler hands take up the work, that the murder w \ a-LJ! f
place in the annals of crime as one of the unsolved mysteries

THE SPIRIT OF THE UNION.
Respectfully dedicated to Lincoln Post, No. 1, G. A. R in re
tion of its manly conduct to tne handful of "Rebs." pavine- thHÂ° 1
diers' last tribute to the hero of the war.
The following- patriotic poem was read by Major Otey the well knnwr,
Confederate officer, at the high jinks of the Bohemian Club eighteen 1
ago. Aside from its loyal ring, it is interesting by reason of its D lh!
which is fulfilled in these war times. tS *> ro P h ecy.
Along the Rappahannock two mighty armies lay;
One displayed the loyal blue, the other wore the gray
Yet the sentries hailed each other, when the autumn day was donÂ«
Then brother asked for brother, and father asked for son
For in that mighty struggle that reft our native land
How many brave hearts perished beneath a kindred hand!
How many loving mothers at night knelt down to pray
That God would watch their boys In blue, and save the ones In gray!
And here beside the river, with its peaceful flowing tide.
The sons of a loving mother were camped on either side.
Who spoke of home and kindred across the tranquil stream
While their eyes put on a far-off look, like people in a dream.
Their voices, too, grew tender, as they spoke of bygone years-
And every word was freighted with almost boyish tears.
At length the words were uttered: "Is mother alive and well?"
"She Is!" "Thank God!" across the stream in tender accents fell.
And thus they, talked of boyhood, as on the banks they lay-
While far off in the glinting the bands began to play.
One played "Off to Dixie," to fight with Massa Lee;
Another played "The Starry Flap " from the mountains to the sea.
And then the heart's full tribute went up to heaven's dome,
As the bands along the line swelled out "Home, Sweet Home."
Then from those mighty armies, in a chorus wild and grand.
Rang out, "Three cheers for horne â God bless our native iand!"
Their honest eyes grew brighter, while the welkin rang with cheers;
Their faces (not less manly) became bedewed with tears
Wore that night a happier look, as they grasped each other's hand.
In that noble first-born Union, love for their native land.
A Union that's grown stronger with them through changing yearn-
That some review with honest pride, and others see through tearsâ
A Union firm cemented throughout the land to-day
For the boys in blue are building homes for those who wore the gray.
And In our old Virginia home our mother wears to-day
A knot of ribbons on her breast, of colors blue and gray,
Beside her sits a veteran, who fought with Robert Lee,
While his brother's bones are resting where Sherman reached the sea.
But when you speak of Union her face lights up with pride
And her hands rest on the curly heads that are playing at her side
And she tells of her dead hero, with a mother's fond regret
And of her rebels, little boys. "Who'll protect the Old Flag yet."
LEES AND HIS STAR CHAMBER.
Alameda Encinal, July 26.
It is a good thing that there is one
paper In San Francisco which has the
courage to attack the star chamber and
secret inquisition methods of Chief Lees.
The "small book" and the secret cells of
"the tanks," wherein men are locked up
for days at a time without being allowed
to communicate with friends, have for
years been a disgrace to the Police De
partment of San Francisco. Like the
"sweating" system to which suspects are
subjected in order to ascertain what the
astute police are unable to discover of
their own motion, they have no founda
EX-CONFEDERATE.
ton in law or decency, and it is high
time that these medieval methods were
relegated to the dark ages. They misjht
have been excusable in Australia^ where
Lees is said to have had h!s first police
experience, l.ut they are out of place in
this day and generation. The Call is de
serving of great credit for having at
tacked one of the worst abuses in San
Francisco.
THE FIRST DOUBLE-DECKER.

ROOT KNOT AND BLUE STONEâ
Henry A. Brainard, editor of the Pa
cific Tree and Vine, San Jose, writes as
follows on the subject of blue stone solu
tion in the treatment of root knot: "I
write to caution against the use of blue
stone solution in the treatment of root
knot except when the trees are perfectly
dormant. If applied to a freshly cut sur
face of the root while the tree has leaves
the solution is taken into the circulation
. and many trees have thus been killed.
The Bordeaux mixture, composed of a
solution of blue stone and milk of lime,
may be used without harm, even In sum
mer, and it appears to have a good ef
fect." â  j
WASHINGTON'S BIRTHDAYâ L.
D., Camp Merritt, City.! j*.* the time that
I George Washington was born, In Febru
| ary, 1732, the old style or Julian calendar
1 was in use by Great Britain. The colo-
I nies being under British rule, that -cal-
I endar prevailed, consequently Washing
| ton was born on the 11th of that month.
In 1751 Great Britain adopted the Grego
rian calendar, and In the colonies as well
as elsewhere where English rule pre
vailed dates were . changed â¢to conform
with the new order of thing's, and as
; tnere were eleven days' difference in the
; calendar at that time, the date of Wash
ington's birthday was ! advanced to the
22d. and it has since then been the recog
nized day of his birth.

ALL THE ARMORCLADS
IN NEED OF REPAIRS
Important Reason Why Watson's
Squadron Has Not Been Sent
Abroad.
NEW YORK, July 26.-The Washing
ton correspondent of the Herald says:
It transpired to-day that an important
reason why the Eastern squadron had
not been dispatched abroad Ik the con
dition of nome of the battle-ships at
tached to it.
It was announced positively at the
department that all of the armorclads,
with the possible exception of the New
York, will be ordered north for the pur
pose of undergoing cleaning and hav
ing repairs made. I was told that it
was the expectation of the administra
tion that the Eastern squadron would
be able to start on its mission by
August 10, provided negotiations for
peace failed.

EARL OF ABERDEEN
SNUBS CONSUL DUDLEY
Turns His Back Upon the American
When an Anglo-Saxon Alliance
Is Broached. s
VANCOUVER, B. C, July There
was a mild sensation in Consular cir
cles to-day over a direct snub adminis
tered to Consul Dudley by the Queen's
representative in Canada, the Earl of
Aberdeen, Governor-General of the Do
i minion, while paying his official visit to
this coast. There were present the Con
suls of Chile, Ecuador, Hawaii, Japan,
Norway and Sweden, Spain, Germany,
France and the United States.
The American Consul made a tentative
remark regarding an Anglo-Saxon alli
ance. Aberdeen fenced the question, say
ing the English-speaking race should be
friends. Consul Dudley fenced for an
opening and again made a courteous re
mark on the alliance subject, which nec
essitated a reply. Aberdeen attempted
to change the subject pointedly, by say
ing:
"Canada has a rich country in the Tv-
The American Consul felt injured, and
pressed his point, when Aberdeen ndget
ted in his hair, turned partly around and,
leaving the United States representative
unanswered, carried on small talk with
other representatives.
Consul Dudley, who is a very courteous
and diplomatic gentleman, is said to have
been much annoyed at the affront. As a
matter of fact. Dudley simply wished to
be pleasant to the Queen's representative.
While Aberdeen, ever hypercautious,
feared to commit himself.

CALLING FOR BIDS FOR
FOUR NEW MONITORS
Limit of Cost Fixed by CongTess Is a
Matter of Regret to Naval
Deoigners.
"WASHINGTON. July 26.â The Navy
Department has Issued an advertise
ment calling for proposals for con
structing by contract the four harbor
defense monitors provided for by the
naval apppropriatlon bill. The bids
will be opened on Saturday, October 10.
It is a matter of regret to the naval
designers that the limit of cost for
these vessels fixed by Congress is so
low as to make it necessary to build
monitors of such small tonnage and of
lighter battery power than is desirable
in these advanced days, but the Con
struction Bureau has managed to pre
pare plans that will, after all, result in
a very serviceable type of harbor-de
fense vessel.

A ROMANCE OF SANTA ROSA
YOUNG WOMAN CASTS ASIDE A
WIDOW'S WEEDS.
Mrs. Edith McCarthy Learns That
the Husband From Whom She
Separated Is Among
the Living.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SANTA ROSA, July 26.â Mrs. Edith
McCarthy, a young woman of good
family in this city, has just learned
that the widow's weeds which she has
worn for months should never have
been donned. She has received the
surprising Information that her hus
band Is yet among tho living, though
his death was believed to have occurred
some time ago.
In \he city of Fresno, on October 14,
1894, Miss Edith Davis, a young lady
well known in Santa Rosa, having
lived here all her life, was married to
George T. McCarthy of San Francisco.
It is said that the marriage did not
prove a happy one, and a short time
later McCarthy went East. Soon word
was received In this city that he was
ill. His wife started for his bedside
at once, but shortly after she had ar
rived at Wheeling, West Virginia, her
destination, a tHe^ram was received by
relatives in this city containing the
news of McCarthy's death. In a few
weeks she returned quietly and alone
to her home in this city.
To-day a lettor was received from
McCarthy stating that he had just seen
a notice of his death published in the
Santa Rosa Press-Democrat, and that
he wished to correct the impression
that he was no longer in the land of
the living. This letter was written from
Pittsburp, Pa.
When ween to-day Mrs. McCarthy
said that upon her arrival in the East
she met her husband. While he un
doubtedly had been ill, he was not se
riously so. She thereupon continued
her journey to New Manchersville, not
far from Wheeling, W. Va., where she
visited relatives. She had been there
but a few days when she received a
telegram from F. W. Adams, whose
name she recognized as that of a per
sonal friend of her husband, stating
that McCarthy was dead, and asking
what disposition should be made of
the body. Upon receipt of this infor
mation she states, she telegraphed to
her relatives in this city.
The letter received from McCarthy
to-day states that his wife left him un
less she started from Wheeling for New
Manchersville. This was after she had
seen him the time she started East, be
lieving him to be very ill. McCarthy's
letter also quotes what he alleges in a
portion of a note his wife sent him
when she starteu from Wheeling. This
extract is to *\e effect that Mrs. Mc-
Carthy did not care to have anything
more to do with her husband.
Mrs. McCarthy has many friends in
this city. Since her return from the
East it has been generally Buppoaed
that she was a v.idow.

Coming to Take Home the Prisoners.
MADRID (via Parts), July 26.â Tho
Transatlantic fleet to bring home the
Santiago prisoners will sail to-morrow.
Captain Aunon, Minister of Marine,
says Admiral Cervera's officers will not
return with the Santiago prisoners, but
their return will be ordered by the
United States Government. He denies
having demanded the expulsion of Don
Carlos from Belgium.
Chinamen Cause a Fire.
CALJSTOGA, July 26.â Fire broke* out
this morning in camp 5 of the Oathill
Quicksilver Mining Company, destroying
the cask and bunk houses. The fire orig
inated from a lighted candle and punks
that were used by Chinese employes to
smoke out the devil. One Chinaman
was badly burned, but will recover.

LONDON, July 27.â A dispatch from

oline Islands.

ADMIRAL MILLER SAILS TO-DAY
TO HOIST THE FLAG OVER HAWAII
RRAR ADMIRAL JOSEPH N.
MILLER will sail to-day at
2:30 p. m.. for Honolulu, on the
rnilser Philadelphia. Ho goes
to Honolulu for the purpose of
formally raising the American
flap over the Hawaiian Islands, when
the annexation proceedings are com
pleted and the transfer is flnaHy made.
When this transaction is to occur Ad
mlral Miller is unable to say. It will,
however, take place in the near future.
The Hawaiian commissioners are to
sail from San Francisco on August 10.
and nothing definite will be done until
after their arrival at Honolulu. It If
expected also that the First New York
Regiment, under Colonel Barber, who
is to be the iÂ»^t commandant, will be
landed at Honolulu on or before that
time, thus enabling the authorities to
make both a land and navul demon
stration.
Admiral Miller takes with him no
special instructions. H<Â» has simply
been directed to proceed to Honolulu to
await orders, which no doubt will be
transmitted to him at the time the
commissioners sail from here.
The Philadelphia, which has been un
dergoing repairs during the hint six
months at Mare Island navy yard,
came down yesterday to the bay and
anchored in the stream opposite Harri-
Bon street.
She'has been put in first-class condi
tion in every respect, and carries a
crew of 880 men.
At > o'clock this morning Admiral
Miller will transfer his flag; from the
Albatross to the Philadelphia, and the
fla.g will be given th^ usual rear-ad
miral's salute of thirteen guns.
lieutenant Commander Curtis of
the Albatross will, during Admiral Mli
ler's absence, have command of the
auxiliary forces, including the revenue
cutters and tugs.
The Adjutant General's office at
Washington now makes the admission
that every State has filled its quota of
troops under each call of th.- President.
The inaccurate reports emanating from
the War Department last week provok
ed a storm of indignation throughout
the country- The misleading state
ments concerning California were so
quickly corrected by the official records
from this State that the Adjutant 'Gen
eral In Washington, was no doubt
prompted to look further to ascertain
if other States had not been misrepre
sented in the figures given to the Asso
ciated Press. In looking up the records
he found that all the troops called for
had been enrolled but that some of the
States had not officially reported the
enrollment.
The official order directing: the move
ment of troops from Camp Merritt to
the Presidio la at last promulgated.
The departure of regiments from Rich
mond will begin to-morrow, when the
Seventh California will break camp
and-.march to. the reservation.

26.â There is . not a .day now which does

THE NEW TROOPSHIP SCANDIA.
Uncle Sam Is to Have a Permanent Fleet of Transports on the Pacific, and the Newly
Purchased Scandia and Arizona Are to Be Fitted Out In the Very Best of Style for
That Purpose. They Will Be Followed by the Fine Big Steamers Athenian and
Tartar, Now at Puget Sound, and Probably Two Others. These, With the Collier 3
Brutus and Nero, Will Make a Fleet with Which 10,000 Troops Could Be Moved to
Any Part of the World at a Three Days' Notice.
The sight of 260,000 fixed ball cartridges,
over 200 to the man, being hauled Into
camp to-day excited curiosity among the
visitors and showed the men that they
were gradually. If slowly, drawing \o the
â¢day when they would be r-alled upon to
do their share of the fighting. The con
signment come 1000 In a box and arf
SS^O-graln ball with solid head. Most of
the supply is from the Winchester Arm
Company, but a little Is from the United
Staf-s Cartridge Company. The ammuni
tion is stored in the adjutant's head
quarters, making the place quite warlike.
The Army Christian Commission tent
has not yet been moved from its position
In the old camp, much to the regret v!
the unlisted men. Each night the guard
line is extended so as to inclose the tent
while the evening service la being held,
but this does not allow of Its use by the
men for writing and reading purposes.
Chaplain Miller Is trying to secure a lo
cation within the guard lines for the tent,
iso it will be open at all hours for the use
of the men. Rev. R R. Lloyd spoke at
the tent to-night, while D. Kdwards will
speak to-morrow evening. H. J. McCoy
â will be present Thursday while the mem
bers of the regiment will give a pro
gramme Friday and Chaplain Miller will
occupy the tent Saturday.
Private Bush. Company F. Oakland, fell
to the ground senseless while drilling this
morning. He was carried to the hospital
by the hospital corps, and did not regain
consciousness until late this evening.
Over-exertion In the heat caused his
trouble, but the surgeon does not con
sider the case at all serious. One of the
men was overheated yesterday, hut is all
right to-day. The pneumonia patient is
able to be about, while the case which
threatened to be measles proved to be
only a serious heat rash with some fever.
He is doing finely and will soon he dls- !
charged as cured.
The guard detail was materially in- j
creased to-night. It consisted of: Cap- !
tain H. M. Simpson, Company <J. Ala- |
meda, officer of the day; lieutenant C. H. |
Holmes, Company E, Santa Rosa, officer >
of the guard; Lieutenant P. M. Miller, \
Company I, Grass Valley, super officer of
the guard: two sergeants., six corporals, ;
.sixty-three private's and twelve supernu- |
merarieg. The latter are to take the i
place of any of the regular guards who ,
may from any cause be detained from i
service when their tour of duty comes
around.

ton and Lieutenant Surgeon I). C. Mc-

HONOLULU GARRISON.
Colonel Barber Expects That
the First New York Will Sail
Early Next Week.
In an Interview with a Call reporter
yesterday Colonel Barber of the First
New York Regiment Bald: "My regi
ment in it? entirety will leave
here on the first of next week
for Honolulu to do garrison duty
upon that Island. The command will sail
by August 3. Three- transports have been
secured, and the regiment will embark
upon them. They will be ready the last
of this week, and they are the Lakme,
North Fork and the Charles Nelson. The
three vessels will accommodate 1330
men."
<'..lonel Barber stated that he would
leave for Honolulu on the St. Paul, which
sails on Friday, as it was the wish of
General Merriam that he should go ahead
of his regiment, for the purpose of Belect
lng a place for its encampment. The First
Xew York will, therefore, be under the
rummand of Lieutenant Colonel Stacpole.
These vessels mentioned by Colonel
Barber will not be accepted until they
have been thoroughly Inspected by the
officers of the Government, and fuiind to
be in every respect worthy. From other
than a military source the Call obtains
this view uf the ships said to be engaged
to transport the First New York:
"The first of the New York troops were
to have sailed iast Monday, but not a
steamer is us yet ready to sail. It ap-
I>t-;u* that sti-am schooners are to be
used, and Just how the soldiers will like
a trip to the tropics in vessels built for
the lumber trade remains to be seen.
Some of the vessel! named as likely to
go have been in the Klondike trade," but
a trip north is a very different thing to a
voyage to the southern fieas.
"Among the vessels mentioned as trans
ports are the Charles Nelson. Samoa,
Lakme and North Fork. The latter ves
sel Is now being fittt-d out cis 'a troop
ship.' while the Lakme will bo due from
the sound to-day, and the Charles Nel
son and Samuti are expected any time
now. The Lakme will have to be over
hauled, as when she arrived at Seattle
from St. Michael her decks were said to
be leaking. The Samoa is a small ves
sel and will have to be built up, while
the <"har!es Nelson is ready for service.
For the Klondike trade the fleet was all
right, but as troopships they will not be
able to handle more than !W> or 600 men."
It is now almost definitely settled that
the corps of engineers will accompany
the New York Regiment. A portion of
the engineers may be transported on otic
of the ships secured to carry Colonel
Barber's regiment to Honolulu. Major
Rice of the California Heavy Artillery is
very hopeful that the two batteries now
here under his command may be sent to
Honolulu, while It is also very highly
probable that the Wyoming (Alger) Light
Artillery will be given a chance to demon
strate its usefulness by being sent to
Honolulu. These two commands are
ready and equipped for duty, and the men
have been drilling constantly for the past
three months, until they have become
nearly perfect In their drills.
Captain Stoats of thp First New York,
the recruiting officer of the regiment, has
been very busily at work for the past
few days, with the result that he" has
recruited the regiment up to its required
strength, with the exception of about six
men, and these he hopes to have by to
day or to-morrow.
SEVENTH CALIFORNIA.
The Regiment Is Ordered
From Camp Merritt to the
Presidio.
The Seventh California, under Colonel
Berry, will move Its camp from Camp
Merritt to the Presidio to-morrow, and will
go into camp upon the cavalry parade.
Just between the New York regiment and
the Alger Battery. Yesterday a force of
workmen were busy laying the neces
sary pipes to supply the camp with water,
and by the first of next week nearly all
of the forces now at Camp Merritt will
have been moved to the Presidio.

A PERMANENT FLEET.
Transports to Be Fitted Out in
the Best Manner for Carry
ing Troops and Supplies.
T'nrle Sam will soon have a permanent
fleet of transports on this coast. They
are being secured one and two at a tlmo,
and they are all to be fitted out in the
very bost manner. The Arizona and Scan
ilia, now in port, are to be turned Into
model transports, and the Athenian and
Tartar, now in Fuget Sound, will be their
equals when Captain Horace Z. Howard
has finished with them. All of these ves
sels are to be fitted with the Turner pat
ent Imnk. nnd everything possible ia to
be done for the comfort of the troops.
; The Athenian and Tartar were owned
! by the Union Steamship Company of
! England, but were under charter to the
Canadian Pacific Railroad, and have been
! engaged in the Alaska trade. Both of
j them are fitted with refrigerating ma
i cnlnery, and as the Arizona Is slmilarlv
supplied and the Scandia has large ice
I chambers, there will be no difficulty In
; ( nrrylng < nough fresh meat for the voy
] age and also a supply of California mut-
I ten for the boys in camp at Manila. The
; Athenian was built in 1881, and is 3582 tona
I gross and 24-10 tons net burden. She Is
365 feet long, 45 feet 8 inches broad and 23
feet deep. The Tartar was built in 1883
: and is 4425 tons gross and 27tÂ» tons net
; burden. She is 378 feet 5 inches long 47
feet 2 Inches broad and 30 feet S inches
deep. Both vessels are about the same
size as the Scandia, and, like her will
accommodate 1500 soldiers. The Arizona
cÂ»uld be fitted out to carry 1800 troops
but considerable of her space is to be
used for freight, and she will only carry
the same number of troops as the others
There wan trouble on both the Scandia
and Arizona yesterday. The German
crew on the former vessel refused duty
| as soon as the German flag wan hauleil
down, and in consequence the food for all
i hands had to be brought aboard from res
! tauraius along the front. Herman Fuk
! Mann, the baker, was the first to strike
! According to him, the hoisting of the
i Stars and Stripes was tantamount to a
! breach of the shipping articles, and he
| at once demanded two months' pay and
! transportation back to Hamburg. The
| cooks, sailors, messboys and firemen fol-
I lowed suit, and then everybody took a
day off and proceeded to form a close ac
quaintance with American beer. The cap
tain could get nothing dune, and had to
hire thirty longshoremen to take his own
and his owner's property ashore. Last
night all hands were paid off, and to-day
they will start for New York on their way
back to Gi-rraany.
Captain Crosky, late chief officer of the
Pennsylvania, 1b temporarily in command
of tho Scaiidla, and (.'hief Engineer
French, late of the Pacific Mail employ,
is in charge of the engine room. When
the Mariposa arrives from Australasia
to-day the chancea are that Mr. Hart,
her chief officer, will be made captain of
the Scandla and Mr. Crosky will be llrst
mate. To-day the big steamer goes on
the drydock and to-morrow she will go
to Angel Island to be thoroughly fumi
gated, after which she will be turned over
to the contractor* to be fitted out.
On the Arizona the trouble was over a
Chinese, and Uncle Sam will now have to
fine himself for neglect of duty. The big
transport has a coolie crew, which she
frocured lv China when she was in the
longkorv*j trade. The Chinese came to
San Francisco on her and are still aboard,
waiting for a vessel to take them to
China. Yesterday morning early one of
the Chinese thought he would pay a visit
to Chinatown. Ho accordingly slipped
past thn guard on the ship arid on the
wharf, and made his escape. When the
crew was mustered in the morning Ah
Sing was mlnalng. and a hue and cry
wfin raised. It was useless, however, and
it will be many a long day before that
Chinese Is sent back to China at Uncle
Sam's expense. Had the vessel been In
charge of her old owners both captain
and ship would have been liable for the
escape of the coolie, but now Uncle Sam
has no one to blame but himself.
TRANSPORT ST. PAUL.
Third Battalion, South Dakota
Volunteers, Will Em
bark To-Morrow.
On Thursday afternoon the Third Bat
talion of the First South Dakota, the re
cruits of the Thirteenth Minnesota Volun

unteers will go aboard the transport St.

Headquarters Department of California, San
Francisco. July 25, IS9B.
Lieutenant Colonel Lee Stover, commanding

United states Volunteers, through commanding

portation will be made by the quartermasters

at 2 o'clock p. m. Thursday, the 28th Inst.

Paul fall out before sailing, their- places may

Captain Read Accidentally Shot
in the Leg â Orders for Cap
tain James G. Blame.
Captain George Read, commanding
Company A of the First Tennessee, is
Buffering from a severe bullet wound in
the fleshy part of the right leg.
Captain Read had his company at the
rifle pits yesterday morning, and while
his men were exercising their Spring
fields Captain Read and Lieutenant Al
Law, Company C, were practicing with
their revolvers.
Law's weapon got out of order, and
while trying to fix it In some manner it
was discharged, the 45-caHber bullet
plowing through the calf of Captain
Read's right limb.
The captain is now confined to his
quarters, but expects to report again for
duty within the week.
Private John Dale, Company H, First
South Dakota, who was accidentally shot
through the right lung a month ago, is
now almost recovered from his wound.
He â will be honorably discharged from
the service, as his injury will leave him
physically disqualified for his duties.
Much to the regret of his co-workers.
Captain Lee Linn, chief commissary of
subsistence for Camp Merritt, is relieved
from that duty and assigned to work In
the Philippines.
Captain Linn, besldeÃ being a veteran
In the commissariat, is a journalist of
thirty years' experience, having been ac
tive In the newspaper fields of Indiana
and Texas. His unusual ability and kind
ly courtesy have won for him the rank
and high position he now occupies under
the Government.
Captain I). Voorhes, a son of the late
Senator Dan Voorhes of Indiana, will
doubtless be assigned to the vacancy.
Captain James G. Blame has been re
lieved from duty as assistant adjntnnt
general on General King's staff and will
Proceed to Manila on the transport St.
Paul.
Lieutenant James K. Polk, First Ten
nessee, hns been assigned to the duties
laid down by Captain Blame.
Private Dubbs, Seventh California, who
died from pneumonia on Sunday, was
buried yesterday at the Presidio.
Captain Klllan, in command of the First
Nebraska recruits, has got his men in
splendid shape, and this afternoon at 2
o'clock will put them through an exhibi
tion drill on the carriage concourse at the
pnrlc.
The equipment of Captain Klllan's com
mand will be completed this week.
Six men were detailed yesterday from
the Eighteenth Infantry to duty as ad
ditional guard for the transnort Scandia.
The remains of Private Lion Hull. Com
pany D, First South Dakota, were buried
yesterday in the Presidio National Cem
etery.
SONS OF VETERANS.
Ready to Step Into the Harness
Should a Third Call
Be Issued.
OAKLAND, July 26.â Sons of Vet
erans and their friends throughout the
State ure organizing military companies
to answer the next rail for troops. The
Governor has granted permission to the
Sons of Veterans to drill and parade
under arms, and the division commander.
Colonel Charles L. Pierce, has author
ized the formation of these companies
and hopes to complete the organization
of an entire regiment.
E. D. Baker Camp No. 5 of this city
has just Issued the following circular in
accordance with the division command
er's, request:
Amid all the hurry and worry In consequence
of our country's war with Spain Americanism,
patriotism and all the kindred Urns have taken
new lift) anil Rive promise of developing most
wonderful results, and while thousands of our
countryman have donned the blue and gone
forth to fight for their country In response to
their own individual loyalty to "Old Glory'
and all she represents, thousands more of us
are ready to follow their lead.
And while willing to do our part and bear
our share In the strife it Is only proper and
right that we should be ready to step into the
harness and without delay be prepared to be
gin active work Immediately after muster In
case we are so fortunate as to be called upon.
To this end it has seamed fitting to the under
signed to call together enough young men of
the right stuff and begin to "whip" ourselves
into military shape. :â .'â â¢*- ;^a^'- . Â£-,;y
We already have nucleus on which to build
and most heartily request you and your eligi
ble friends to Join us In the Interesting work.
Captain C. T. Poulter of Company A, Fifth
Regiment, has volunteered the use of his com
pany's armory as well as their talents.
Our boys will meet next Monday evening- at
Twelfth and Webster streets, southeast corner
of Exposition building, and if forty or fifty
assemble we will march to A's armory and Join
them in, their evolutions.
Repeating our earnest request that you Join
us in the formation of a military company. and
promising to do our share for the success of
the organization, wo are, sir, our obedient
servants. -
C. A. TABER,
. . J. A. SANDS.
J. F. SUMMERS,
F. C. SHIPLEY.
L D. MANNING,

Merited Promotion.
Prpss dispatches from Guantanamo
Bay contain this item: "First. Sergeant
of Marines Klnne of the New York went
north yesterday on the Texas to get his
commission as second lieutenant of ma
rines. This is almost the only case of
such promotion on record in this branch
of the service."
The young officer mentioned in the fore
going dispatch Is Frank A. Klnne, son of
Colonel C. Mason Kinne of this city. He
enlisted as a private of marines six years
ago and has Rained his promotion step
by step on his merits. He sailed with
Captain Watson on the San Francisco
six years ago and was with that ship for
several years at the various stations in
Europe. ' He has been on Admiral Samp
son's flagship New York since the begin
ning of the present war, participating In
all the actions In which the flagship was
engaged.

Clay & Co. Those holding reserved seat

by the private door on Lark in street, to
the south of the mnin entrance.

REGISTRAR BIGGY
AND PARKER JONES
QUESTIONS THE APPOINTMENT
OF THE CLERK.
It Is Probable That the Courts Will
Be Asked to Decide the Elec
tion Commission Wrangle.
The appointment of Parker Jones as
clerk of the Board of Election Commis
sioners will be stubbornly contested by
Registrar Biggy. The Registrar says that
he will appeal to the Treasurer to refuse
the payment of Jones' demand, and, if
necessary, will carry the case Into ihe
courts.
When the commission met yesterday
morning in executive session to continue
the work of selecting precinct registra
tion officers. Registrar Biggy appeared
before the board to protest against the
appointment of Jones. He contended that
the clerical work of the commission
should be performed by either the Regis
trar or one of his deputies. Biggy took
the stand that it was an unnecessary
waste of public money, to employ a clerk
for the eommisÃion when the Registrar
waa fully able to attend to the duties.
The Commissioners, headed by City and
County Attorney Creswell, announced
that Jones had been retained by them
and that he would" act as secretary dur
ing its present session.
Registrar Biggy then withdrew from
the meeting room and the Commissioners
resumed the work of selecting the pre
cinct registration officers. They will con
clude their labors to-day, and at a meet
ing to be held to-morrow morning will
make public the names of those selected.
The Registrar is confident that he will
be able to prevent the payment of Jones'
demand. He clt"es as authority the case

A Suspicion That the Deaths of the
Captain and Mate of the Ken
ilworth Were Not DvÂ»
to an Accident.
Tho cruiser Philadelphia came down
from Mare Island navy-yard and an
chored in the stream yesterday. Soon
afterward Rear Admiral Miller's pennant
run up and a salute of thirteen guns
fired. The warship is all ready for her
trip to Honolulu, and will probably get
ti way to-morrow or as soon as her fresh
provisions have been put aboard. On his
arrival at the capital of Hawaii, Admiral
Miller and the Commissioners who will
go down on the cruiser with him will
hoist the Stars and Stripes, and the lsl
ands will thenceforth form part of the
great American republic.
Among the passengers who went away !
on the steamer Gaelic for Hawaii yester- !
day was ex-Queen Lilluokalani. She was
accompanied by Dr. \V. English, her phy- i
Blclan; Joseph Heb-lehue, her private sec- !
loncl John Richardson.
Other notables who sailed on the Gaelic
â were Lee Yuk Wn and I.cc Yuet Fung.
AY a is president of the Ning Tung, the
biggest and n;"St powerful of the Chi- j
nese Six Companies, and tung Is one of 1
the largest buyers of California products
in all Hawaii. There was an immense
concourse of Chinese down to see their
fellow-countrymen away.
The gunboat Iroquois -.formerly the tug
Fearless) now has company. The Vigi
lant came down from Mare Island yester
day and the Active will follow to-day. All
three vessels are now ready tor sea, and
the Vigilant may be ordered out for a
cruise at any moment. The Fearless is
w the Tacnma t>' Manila, and the
Active may be sent to Honolulu as a dis
patch boat.
E. T. Colnon, president of the State
Board of Harbor Commissioners, is about
to take a well-earned vacation. In order
to gc-t away from all business cares and
worries for at least ;â . month he has
shipped as an "ordinary seaman" aboard
the American clipper ship R. D. Rice.
The vessel is going to 1 !>e sound to load
for tho islands, and Mr- ' olnon will leave
her as soon as she dock.' 1 at Tacoma. Of
course, he is going as the guest of Cap
tain Carver, and the signing of articles Is
a matter of form.
J M M. Cabe, buyer for Redington &
Co., died In the Harbor Receiving Hospi
tal yesterday. He was subject to epilep
tic fits and fell in one la< t Monday night.
l>r. Deas attended to him, but he never

The Kenllworth added a little more
notoriety to her record as a "very hard
ship" while last In Hilo, Hawaii. She
had a very hard crew, anyway, but, as
usual, there were a few who were as
bad as deep water men come; there were
Eeven who showed their hands soon after
the ship arrived In the harbor. They re
fused to work in port, and attempted
to desert, but the master appealed to the
American Consul and they were arrested
ly ihc loon I police and returned to the
custody of the ship, vowing to have ven
geance on the officers the first opportun
ey had. They were placed In irons
nrown in the sail locker. But they
did not remain there long before they
: c to a set of new sails, which were
mostly consumed. The Consul took a
hand after numerous appeals from Cap
tain Baker and the mutineers were turned !
over to the local police again. They had '
another trial and were remanded to the
iy of Captain Baker. This time he
Incarcerated them in a more secure place
j'roniised to starve them until they ]
turned to. Under this diet their senses !
returned and they were liberated. Soon :
ward three made their escape in one
of the shore boats. The remaining four,
S no other chance of escape, and
the ship being loaded, jumped over the
Fide, thinking to make the shore, a good
milÂ© and a half, unaided, but they were
drowned, as the current was strong and
the wind blowing hard. It Is now be
lieved, by those familiar with thp cir
cumstances that the mutineers who failed
to escape vowed to fire the ship, and the
report of her lorn off the coast of Chile
is a natural sequence.

AN EMBASSADOB'S SYMPATHY.
Horace Porter Writes in Begard to
the La Bourgogne Disaster.
United States Embassador Horace Por
ter, under the date of July 7, wrote the
following letter to Eugene Periere, presi
dent of the Compagnie Generale:
Dear Sir: It was only this morning I
learned of the disaster to La Bour
gogne. in which perished so many brave
men, and I hasten to address you these
few lines to tell you I share with you in
the painful emotion which this great ca
