MANAGER VINING
IS VERY IRATE
SAYS THAT THE STTPEBVISOBS
ACTED ABBITBABrLY.
He Declares That a Fight Will Be
Made Against Bemoving the
Horse Car Bails on Mar
ket Street.
The Market Street Railway Company
will, if General Manager Vlning Is au
thority on the Intended action of the cor
poration, fight the municipality in the
courts to prevent the removal of the
horse car tracks on Market street, be
tween Second and Sixth.
"The Market Street Company," Bald
Vlning yesterday, "nas been carrying out
tue terms of its franchises. The action
taken by the Supervisors on Monday or
dering the removal of the horse carrails
was. to say the least, very arbitrary,
lhat method of declaring a franchise in
valid is a bad one and can never succeed.
Our company will do all that the law re
quires of it, but it cannot be forced
to pay the cost of repaying Market
street. The city has already ac
cepted the street and is, therefore, re-
Bponsible for any changes made in the
matter of pavement. The city claims that
when the horse car lines were consoli
dated with our electric and cable sys
tems the original franchises for horse
cars went out of effect. We claim that
such Is not the case, and that the fran
chises still remain In force and effect
while we live up to the terms of the fran
chises. Under these circumstances the Su
pervisors have no right to order the re
moval of the horse car rails and we will
stand by our rights."
Mayor Phelan, on the other hand, says
that the best legal authorities In the city
declare that the Market Street Railway
Company has no claim to the street now
covered by the horse car rails. "I am
determined." said he yesterday, "to force
the issue. If the company does not com
mence the work of repaying the street
and removing its horse car traces at the
expiration of the ten-day limit action will
be commenced by the city. I do not know
what steps will be taken, but this I do
knowâ the proper steps will be taken
and taken wtih vigor. The railroad com
pany will be forced to conform to the
laws of the city."
TWO WOMEN FIGHT
IN THE COURT ROOM
BOTH LOVED FRANK DANEBI
AND WERE DECEIVED.
He Gave the Earring's of Madame
Georgette to Emila Capelli, and
a Hair-Pulling Match
Followed.
Madame Georgetta and Em!la Capelli
are to-day phy on hair and sore In body.
They had a battle In Justice Groezinger's
court yesterday afternoon, but owing to
the Interference of mutual friends no
other decision than a draw could be
given.
The fight was not for a purse. The tro
phy was a pair of diamond earrings and
the affections of Frank Daneri. Both
â¢women have a fondness for Daneri, but
did not know that they were rivals until
yesterday, when they met In the court
room. Madame Georgetta had sued Ca
pelli for the earrings, claiming: that they
were her property. Capelli replied by
stating that the jewels belonged to her,
being a present from Daneri. It was Just
at that moment that the battle began.
Madame Georgetta In an instant was af
ter Capelli. They did not wait for the
call of time. Both women went to the
center with fire in their eyes and a de
termination to fight to the death. They
clinched and when separated both had
a handful of hair to mark the first round.
Without taking the minute rest allowed
under Queensberry rules they went at one
another again. There was another hair
clinch and shin-kicking match. The
si>potators protested against a continu
ance of the match, as neither of the con
testants was fighting fair.
Madame Georgetta, full of anger but
minus many golden tresses was led out
<>f the courtroom and taken away by her
frk-mls. Madame Capelli was escorted
from the impromptu prize ring five min
utes lat<Â»r and the "white winged angel
of peace" resumed her place on the head
of the "wingless angel" on the dome of
the new City Hall tower.
Justice Groezinger will render his de
cisionânot on the merits of the fight
but as to the ownership of the earrings,
when the janitor removes the "evidences"
of the "mill" from the courtroom.
KICKED OUT OF A RESTAURANT.
Complaint of Charles Heath, a Col
ored Man From Oakland.
Charles Heath, a colored man, living
at Golden Gate Station, Oakland, was
the complaining witness in a battery
case in Judge Mogan:s court yesterday
morning. The defendant, was Spaso Java.
vich, who keeps a restaurant on the water
front.
Heath testified that he went into the
restaurant Monday and ordered a cup of
coffee, and chops. After finishing his
meal he offered 10 cents in payment, when
Javovich grabbed him by the coat collar
and kicked him out of the place.
Javovich a"sked for a continuance until
to-day, when he will present his side of
the case.
B. B. Roberts ActÂ«d Fairly.
The statement in last Sunday's Call |
that B. B. Roberts, agent for the Tesla
Coal Company, had taken a five-dollar
gold piece from two newsboys and re
fused to return it until arrested is now
satisfactorily explained. The facts in the
case are that Mr. Roberts, suspecting the
boys had received the coin from some one i
who thought it a nickel, took the money
with the intention of giving It to a po
liceman. One of the officers to whom he
offered the gold piece refused to take it
but told Mr. Roberts, whose card he took
to give it to Captain Splllane. Subse
quently Mr. Roberts saw the two boys
in conversation with two policemen in
a saloon. He voluntarily went up and
giving his version of the affair, turned
the money over to the officers, who ex
pressed their belief that he had done the
proper thing.
New Mining Corporation.
Articles of incorporation of the Plym
outh Rock Mining Company were filed
yesterday. The capital stock of the cor
poration 18 $100,000, of which $50,000 has
been subscribed. The directors are J. A.
Parsons. J. A. Sanborn, C. E. Brown, "W.
H Murphy and C. D. Bailey.
Exempt From War Tax.
Secretary Godchaux of the Board of
Health received a communication from
Collector Lynch yesterday informing him
that the war tax was not imposed on cer
tificates recording either birtns or deaths.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, JULY 27, 1898.
REAL ESTATE
AT A LOW EBB
A Week Brings Little
Encouragement.
THE SALES SMALL AND FEW
PBOGBESS IN BUILDING FOBMS
A STBIKING CONTBAST.
The Beview of the Becords â New
and Becent Building Contracts
and General Bealty
Notes.
The condition of the real estate mar
ket t\a.s Improved but little during the
past week. Few, If any, sales of more
than ordinary importance have been
negotiated and the general current of
the realty stream seems to be in a state
of stagnation. The brokers, however,
content themselves with and abide by the
hope that the present dullness cannot
last much longer and that prosperous
times will inevitably follow the summer
monthsâ the dullest period in tho entire
year.
Building operations are steadily In
creasing and form a striking contrast
to the lack of business progress in real
estate. Many contracts, both for large
and for email amounts, have been let
during the past seven days with a good
prospect for more in the future.
The record for large mortgages Is
small, there being few aggregating In
value more than $10,000. Releases and re
conveyances, as shown by the records are
likewise few, and not for unusually largo
pledges.

O F yon Rheln reports the completion of
his 'buildings on Third and Jessie streets,
which have been under construction for the
last six months. The fact that before the
buildings passed from the contractor to the
owner the principal portions have been leased,
each for a term of years to responsible parties,
seems to prove that an up-to-date structure in
a central locality, even In these times, need not
go without tenants in San Francisco.
The following are the lessees of the three
stores: Llndh the tailor; Pacific Electric Mo
tor Company and Messrs. Hurtzlg & Sehmelz.
The last named firm are druggists.
The upper part, used as a lodging house and
consisting of forty-six rooms, has been leased
to r-lark W. Phillips.
All that remains unrented are two basements
and negotiations are pending for the leasing
of these two; they are eleven feet high, are
furnished with sidewalk elevators and nave
been so constructed as to be as light as the
stores above them.
Yon Rheln & Co. also report that seven flats
and two stores, erected under their supervision,
on the northwest corner of Geary and Jones
streets, are nearly finished and that six of the
flats and one store have been rented before the
completion of the building. The corner store
has been leased to a pharmacy.
The same firm Is pushing to . ompletfon seven
strictly modern flats on the northwest corner
of Jackson and Jones streets, belonging to Mr.
John Shirley. Although these flats will not be
completed until some time in September, appll

LOCHNER OUT
OF THE TANKS
Coroner's Jury Fonnd
Nothing Against Him.
MURDER MYSTERY UNSOLVED
WOMAN "STBANGLED BY PABTY
OB PABTIES UNKNOWN."
Police Are Now Industriously Look
ing for the Missing Husband
and the Stolen
Watch.
After being imprisoned for nine days
with no charge against him Joseph Loch
ner, degenerate, who was suspected of
murdering Sadie Carpenter in the Hub
bard House, Is now free. He was re
leased yesterday immediately after the
Coroner's jury brought in a verdict that
the woman "came to her death by stran
gulation at the hands of party or parties
unknown." The verdict was approved by
the Coroner.
When the inquest wajs resumed at 9
o'clock yesterday morning Lochner took
the stand and completed the tale of his
connection with the tragedy, which was
begun the day before. He made a little
speech to the jury before leaving the
stand in which he reviewed the testimony
which had been given at the inquest and
called the attention of the jury to incon
sistencies in the evidence' given by Lizzie
Riley and others. He alluded to the
statement of Mrs. Riley that she heard
some one wearing squeaking shoes leave
Sadie's door and go down the stairs, and
then referred to Night Clerk Prieur's
testimony that he saw no one leave the
house. Lochner said he often came home
late at night and saw the clerk dozing in
his chair and on other occasions Prieur
was nowhere in sight. He denied that
he had ever quarreled with Mrs. Carpen
ter and wished the jury to take cogni
zance of the fact that her watch, money
and some of her wearing apparel are
missing. His final wurds were:
"Gentlemen, I am a law-abiding citi
zen, and innocent of any crime."
Coroner Hawkins and one of the jurors
cross-examined Lochner, but he made no
admissions which would in any way con
nect him with the murder. He was then
excused.
Thomas McDonough testified that he
went to his room in the Hubbard House
about 1 a. m. on the night af the murder,
but he did not hear or see anything un
usual. "Darby" Glynn followed him and
told of Lochner's visit to the Bellevue
saloon, when he told the bartender and
himself that Mrs. Carpenter had been
found dead in hfer bed. Deputy Coroner
took the stand and said that he
had found Sadie's purse in the bureau
drawer and opened it in the presence of
Lochner. ((Lochner denied this.) Sol
dier Carsten, who had been a friend of
Sadie's for two years, was sworn, but
he could shed no light on the mystery of
her death. George Weissgerber, barten
der at the Bellevue saloon, gave testimo
ny similar to that of Glynn.
George Dolan and D. W. Shea, two new
witnesses, swore that they were in Sa
die's room about two months ago and
had a conversation regarding Lochner.
She told them that she was afraid of a
crazy Dutchman who occupied a room
connecting with hers, and showed them
the curling irons with which she bolted
the door. She told them that he was
jealous of her and became wild when he
saw her talking to other men.
This concluded the testimony. The Jury
retired and remained out fifteen minutes,
returning with the verdict.
After approving the findings the Coro
ner discharged the jury- As they were
leaving thoir seats Lochner jumped up
and said: "Gentlemen, I thank you."
"You needn't," replied one of the jur
ors, as he passed him, with a loathing
look.
Lochner was taken hack to the City
Prison and about an hour afterward was
released by Captain Seymour, indorsed by
Chief Lees.
The Chief and Captain Seymour had no
fault to find with the Coroner' 3 verdict.
They both admitted that there was no
evidence against Lochner. The depart
ment has resumed and redoubled its ef
forts to get some trace of the missing
watch. A systematic search of the pawn
shops and repair-shops is now being
made. A strenuous endeavor to locate
Carpenter, the mysterious husband, is
also being made.
Yesterday afternoon Mrs. Anderson
called on Captain Seymour and gave Jiim
a list of things which she thinks were
taken from Sadie's room either at the
time or after she was murdered. The
list includes: One brown dress skirt (Mrs.
Carpenter's wedding dress); one silk bed-
Bpre&d, with white hand-made lace bor
der and a silk handkerchief in the cen
ter (Mrs. Carpenter made the spread and
intended to give it to her younger daugh
ter); two night-dresses; one pnir of fÂ»ltÂ«>. - s;
one pair of slippers; two pairs of cotton
hose; a fine tablecloth; two linen towels
and six napkins.
Mrs. Andorson says she is positive Sa
die did not dispose of these things, as she
had them only a few days before her
death and intended to send them to Mon
torey. The mother is also sure that Sa
die had considerable money, because a
few days before her death â¢Vne wrote her
mother to make up a list of groceries
and other things she needed for the home
and the children. At intervals whenever
needed Sadie bought a large supply of
necessities and sent them to her mother.
A DRUMMER'S ILL LUCK.
A. Parker of Los Angeles Seeks Rest
in the Receiving Hospital.
A. Parker, a respectably dressed young
man, walked into the Chief of Police's
office yesterday afternoon and complained
that he was under the hypnotic spell of
a Dr. Pierson of San Luis Oblspo. He
said he had met the doctor in Oakland
and the spell had come over him again.
Corporal Geimann, who is specially de
tailed to look after rrnnks visiting police
headquarters, took Parker to the Receiv
ing Hospital, where a dose of "d. t." mix
ture was administered to him.
After a while Parker said he was a
traveling salesman for the White Manu
facturing Company of Los Angeles, and
he had been all through Lake County
without doing much business. As it was
his trial trip he worried so much over
his lack of success that his mind had
become affected. He forgot all about the
hypnotic story he had told in the Chief's
office, and said all that he wanted now
was rest for his troubled mind.
Fractured Her Skull.
Mrs. Morgan, 221 Fifth street, met with
an unfortunate accident early yesterday
morning. She was going from the bed
room to the kitchen to see if it was time
for her husband to get up when she
missed her footing and fell down a flight
of stairs. She was taken to the Receiv
ing Hospital, where she was treated for
a lacerated wound of the scalp and' pos
sible fracture of the skull.

EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK. July 26.â A combination of
causes was responsible for the awakening of
the stock market to-day from the lethargy
that has held It for nearly two months. The
bears had their innings early In the day, a.Â«
eisted by a lower range of prices for Americans
from London. Later Northwest and its sub
sidiary road, Omaha, had a rally, and from
them the movement broadened and strength
ened all through the railroad list. Strength
â was also radiated from a group of bituminous
r 'ads. The activity and strength In
Eusrar also exercised an Influence In the mar
ket, accompanied thoueh it was by all sorts of
sensational rumors, and very obviously manip
ulated to squeeze the shorts. Peace runors
were renewed In the later dealings and the
announcement of the successful landing of the j
United States forces in Porto Rico also added I
to the buoyant tendency of the trading. But |
the most potential fact in the situation was the
growing certainty that an abundance of money
would continue at the command of the busi- i
ness of this country. Sterling exchange fell !
%c additional in the actual rates and %c in
the posted rates.
The surplus of Idle money was very evident
in the bond market seeking all issues except the
â class of gilt-edged bonds which have risen to
practically prohibitive prices. Total sales, !
$4,305,000.
United States old 4s registered advanced %
and the new 3s (when issued) declined % in
the bid price.
The total sales of stocks to-day were 321,500
shares, including: Atchison preferred, 14,100;
Baltimore and Ohio, 7492: Burlington, 15,300; i
Louisville and Nashville, 5775; Manhattan, 7547;
Reading preferred. 12.075; Missouri Pacific, 3410;

LONDON MARKET.
NEW YORK, July 26.â The Evening Post's
London financial cablegram says: The stock
markets here continued lifeless to-day. The
settlement shows a good account and Is favored
by easy money. Contangoes on Americans
were about 2VÂ» per cent. The general tone of
Americans was good, but investment stocks
were lower on indications of possible dearer
money. The markets were quiet, but the close
was decidedly firm on New York support. The
discount market is rising on the fall of Ameri
can exchange. The question of gold exports
to New York Is freely discussed, some people
affirming that gold may go next month. Such
talk, however, is premature.

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
CHICAGO, July 26.â Weather of the most
favorable character for the spring crop and
reported depression of prices at Liverpool and
London caused a weak feeling here at the
opening. July was much weaker than more
distant deliveries, owing to the freedom of
the movement of new wheat and the conse
quent greater reluctance of millers and ship
pers to continue paying premiums for imme
diate. The weather in the United Kingdom
and Continent was said to be favorable. Har
vesting was reported progressing In Russia
and Koumanla, with prospects of an enormous
yield. The spot price here was down four
cents, and outside markets reported a corre
sponding decline. Futures became heavier un
der that break in the cash market and prices
continued on the downward course until the
close of the session. July closed 3%c lower;
September 2'ic and December l%igl%c down.
Scattered showers and cooler weather start
ed corn lower. Scalpers were disposed to do
considerable selling and some of the promi
nent bulls also sold freely. The country, how
ever, was a good buyer through commission
houses, and a steady feeling waa developed.
The close was %<S%e lower than yesterday.
Oats were almost steady on a good seaboard
demand. September closed %c lower.
Provisions opened easy on the soft feeling
in corn and lower market for hogs. The mar
ket had an appearance of firmness at about
5c decline until near the close, when prices
slumped off on the break in wheat. Pork clos
ed 37^c lower; lard lufiUVs off; and ribs 15Â®
17Hc lower.

deal of Hay In the country for farm use which
would otherwise be sent here- for sale. At
present arrivals are large and the feeling Is
weak in consequence.

NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given In the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day in the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column gives the second tide of
the day, the third time column the third tide
and the last or right hand column gives the
last tide of the day, except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
given are additions to the soundings on the
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (â ) precedes the height,
and then the number given Is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference is the mean of the lower low waters.

NOTICE TO MARINERS.
A branch of the United States Hydrographlc
Office, located in the Merchants' Exchange, is
maintained in San Francisco for the benefit of
mariners without regard to nationality and
free of expense.
Navigators are cordially Invited to visit the
office, where complete seta of charts and sall
ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, and the latest
information can always be obtained regarding
lights, dangers to navigation and all matters
of interest to ocean commerce.
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
building, at the foot of Market street, is hoisted
about ten minutes before noon and dropped at
noon, 120 th meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived each day from the United States Signal
Observatory. Mare Island. Cal.
A notice stating whether tho ball was dropped
on time or giving the error, if any. is published
In the morning papers the following day.
CIIAS. P. WELCH.
Ensign (retired), V s. N.. In chanre.

YOUNG MEN'S LEAGUE WILL
HAVE NEW QUARTERS.
Da-vis and Pardee Clubs Are Now
Getting to Work Ready for
the Primaries Next
Month.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
90S Broadway, July 26.
According: to the apportionment of the
State Central Committee Alameda County
will have sixty-seven votes In the State
convention. These are divided as fol
lows, upon the vote for D. E. McKinlay
for Presidential elector; Forty-sixth As
sembly district, eight delegates: Forty
eeventh, fifteen gates; Forty-eighth,
eight delegates; Forty-ninth, eleven dele
gates; Fiftieth, ten delegates; Fifty-first,
â¢â¢fifteen delegates.
.::!> Central Committee will
week and will discuss plans
for holding the primaries. The Third
District Congressional committee will also
meet this week and arrange for its con
vention at Vallejo.
The Young Men's Republican League,
.which has been very active recently, has
hired a new hall and in future their
meetings will be held at 1159 Washington
street, between Thirteenth and Four
teenth. The hall is being lighted by elec
tricity and tastefully decorated and is
mui'h" larger than the old quarters. On Fri
day evening, August 5. the league will hold
its ,-armlng in its new hall, and all
Kepublicans. are invited to be present.
The speakers will be John A. Brittan, D.
Edward Collins, Giles H. Gray and James
A. Johnson.
There will be a meeting of the Fifth
"Ward Pardee Club at Elite Hall on Fri
day evening.
The Seventh Ward Republican Club
will meet at Castle Hall, Twenty-third
avenue, next Friday evening.
The Second "Ward Davis Club will meet
at Milton Hall Wednesday evening, and
will be addressed by Hon. W. R. Davis,
Hon. Romualdo Pacheco and others.
The Fourth Ward Republican Club met
this evening in People's Hall on Sixth
street, and were addressed by T. V. Eddy
nf San Frai
James Stanley of Mission San Jose, for
many terms Public Administrator of this
ty, has returned. X rom Alaska and
announces himself as a candidate before
the Republican convention for the State
lizatlon.
The Fruitvale Republican Club has been
organized with E. 11. Marwedel, presi

Congressman Hilborn is now at home
and is attending personally to the details
of his campaign for rt-nnmination.
Three Cases of Insanity.
OAKLAND. July 28.â 1t required three
policemen to land Clarence E. Potter, a
17-year-old boy living at 2245 Central ave
nue, Alamnla, (i) the insane ward of the
Receiving Hospital to-day. ' He is very
violent and had threatened to kill his fa
ther, He will be examined at 10 o'clock
to-morrow morning.
Mrs, Louisa Francis, 78 years of age,
of 1928 Poplar street, will be examined
to-morrow at the same time. Hit hallu- ,
cination Is a desire to play in the fire.
William Barry, a colored citizen of
Berkeley, who formerly worked at the
Presidio, was committed to the State As
ylum at Ukiah to-day. He was struck
on the head accidentally and his mania
is to pull up sidewalks and plant sea
weeds in his front yard.
REWARDED FOR HIS SCHOLARSHIP.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, July 26.
Rev. Philip Gralf, M. A., pastor of the First Free Baptist Church of this
city, nan received official notification from the governing board of Bates
conege Lewiston, Me., that he has been honored with the degree or D. Q.
nates college is the foremost s<at of learning of the Free Baptist denom
ination in the Lnlted States, and a degree from its faculty is highly hon
ored. Ihe distinction ha_s been conferred upon Dr. Gralf in recognition of
the range and wealth of his scholarship and the literary ability shown by him
as a preacher, author and theologian. Outside of the definite pastoral sphere
ho has been an Industrious and prominent leader in the Chautaugua move
ment a frequent contributor to magazines and a lecturer. Some of these
have been adopted as standard theology by some Eastern colleges. As a
linguist Dr. Graif is specially known, being a master in the Hebrew, Ara
maic, Greek, Latin, German, French and Italian languages.
ELOPED ON AN
ELECTRIC CAR
Miss Bowers From San
Leandro Is Married.
WIFE OF SAMUEL CROWLEY
VICAR GENERAL GAVE PERMIS
SION TO WED.
Anxious to Avoid Much Ceremony
They Crossed the Bay and
Surprised Their
Friends.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, July 26.
The season of elopements has again
come around In the Interior of the county.
To-day Mr. and Mrs. Samuel L. Hooson-
Crowley sent word from San Francisco
to their parents that they were married
yesterday in St. Mary's Cathedral by Rev.
Father Dempsey.
Yesterday Mr. Crowley. the stepson of
Mr. Ilooson, le-^ Haywards on an electric
car -without telling any one his business.
At San Leandro Miss Frances M. Bowers
joined Mr. Hooson and they crossed over
to San Francisco. The meeting had been
prearranged and two friends had been
tak.'ii Into the confidence of the young
people. They were Dan Sullivan and
Bliss Lily Bowers, sister of me bride. The
quartet," on arrival in San Francisco, at
once procured a marriage license and
started out to find a priest wno would
perform the ceremony. All was not
smooth sailing, for when Father Demp
sey was approached he refused to marry
the young couple and advised them to go
back and be married b'- their parish
priest. Mr. Hooson and Father Dempsey
wore fellow students at St. Mary's Col
lege some years ago, and finally the
father consented to submit the matter to
the Vicar General. The young couple vis
ited Vicar General Prendergast. and on
his authority Father Dempsey performed
the marriage ceremony in St. Mary's
Cathedral yesterday afternoon.
Samuel Crowley, who has been known
nearly all his life by the name of his
stepfather, J. Hooson, is one of tP / ~ best
known young men of Washington Town
ship. He has taken a prominent part in
bringing different fraternal conventions to
Haywards, and has frequently attended
various gatherings aa a delegate from
his town.
"We did not want any unnecessary
ceremony." said Mrs. Crowley this after
noon, "and so we thought it better to
cross the bay and get married as quietly
as possible. I suppose it was an elope
ment, hut Mr. Crowley and I made up
our minds several days ago and took his
friend, Mr. Sullivan, with us for best man,
while my sister came with us and acted
as bridesmaid."
The City's Western Boundary.
OAKLAND, July 26.â The Southern Pa-
I cific Company has refused to pay any
1 tax upon the personal property upon the
â  mole or long wharf beyond the line of
low tide to City Assessor Snow. It does
this in view of the late decision of the
Supreme Court in the water front case,
which held that the city only extended
to the low tide line. The western boun
dary line of the city has always been con
sidered at the three-fathom line. As the
assessment Is on the books It may er.d
in the matter being taken to the courts
and the city's boundary judicially de
termined.
A Question of Ownership.
OAKLAND, July 26.â Patrick Duffy has
filed a suit for a restraining order to
prevent Hugh M. Cameron from remov
ing a house on East Tenth street, near
J'ark way. He claims the property and
house in question, but Mr. Cameron de
clares that the house was moved on the
property by mistake. It was intended to
place the house upon an adjoining lot.
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, JULY 27, 1898.
STRANGE CASE OF POISONING
Miss Haines of Berkeley Died After Four Hours'
Sickness in a House in Which a Man Was
Shot Dead a Short Time Ago.
BERKELEY, July 26.â 1n the Halnes residence In South Berkeley,
Miss Mabel Haines died yesterday after a sickness of four hours with
all the symptoms of poisoning.
The case was so unmistakably one of poisoning that Dr. W. Allen,
the family physician, refused to sign the death certificate, and reported
the case to the Coroner.
Miss Haines was taken sick yesterday afternoon about 2 o'clock.
Dr. Allen, the family physician, found her quite unconscious when he
reached the house shortly before three. She continued in an uncon
scious state for four Jiours, passing away a little after six. Mr. and
Mrs. Haines were away in San Francisco during the greater part of
their daughter's brief illness, but a telegram brought them to her bed
side before the laat moments came.
Dr. Allen waa unable to determine the cause of death at once,
and so deemed a Coroner's Investigation necessary. Deputy Coroner
Streightlf communicated to-day with Coroner Baldwin, who must or
der a post-mortem examination.
It is understood that the family believe some blackberries, of
which the unfortunate girl partook at luncheon, to have been pois
oned. The Coroner said this evening that he would await develop
ments, and could not decide whether an autopey would be held till
later.
Later It was learned that the attending physician certified to the
death as being caused by cerebral hemorrhage resulting In apoplexy,
and brought about by -violent vomiting.
D. E. Allison does not like the statement made about his wife by
Mrs. Haines, mother of the dead trirl, anl to-night he prepared the fol
lowing statement, which Mrs. Haines refused to sign:
"I hereby retract the statement made by me that my daughter
Mabel was poisoned by carbolic acid, nor did I imply that Mrs. D.
Allison was instrumental in administering said poison."
Coroner Baldwin has determined to hold an inquest and autopsy
to-morrow morning. The Allisons are neighbors of the Haines, and it is
alleered that Mrs. Allison was in some way connected with the girl's
taking the poison. Dr. Allen, who attended the girl for a few hours
before her death, signed the death certificate this afternoon.
WEALTHY PIONEER
OF HAYWARDS DIES
HENRY STROBEL SUCCUMBS TO
BLOOD POISONING.
Wide-Awake and Public-Spirited, He
Made Money by Attention to
Business for Thirty
Years.
OAKLAND, July 26-Henry Strobel of
the firm of Strobel, Neudeck & Co. died
at his residence in this city this morn
ing from blood poisoning, superinduced by
a carbuncle. He was one of th<Â» wealth
iest men in the interior of this county,
and had been a resident of the town for
thirty years.
Mr. Strobel had been engaged In the
butcher business most of the time he
lived here, and most of his wealth, esti
mated at $75,000, is in that business. His
estate consists of some realty and per
sonal property. He leaves two sons,
Charles Strobel, with Holbrook, Merrill
& Stetson, and Vincent, who was asso
ciated with his father.
By Mr. Strobel's death the town of
Haywards loses one of its most wide
awake and public spirited men. He often
helped new enterprises to keep upon their
feet, and was always ready to contribute
to anything that would benefit the town.
He was a life long Republican and served
bs Town Trustee with honor and credit.
He was a native of Germany, 62 years of
age. His wife survives him.

Wandered Away From Home.
OAKLAND. July 26.â William Bennett,
for a number of years employed by the
Oakland Creamery, who suddenly disap
peared two years ago and was discovered
near Santa Rosa after two weeks' search
ing has been missing from his home, 152b
Broadway, since last Friday. His wife
fears that he has wandered away as he
did before, when he declared that every
thing was a blank from the time he left
home until his return. She ha* six chil
dren to care for. The only excuse for
.such conduct Is In the fact that four
years ago Mr. Bennett was struck on the
head by a local train, which affected his
brain seriously.

COLOR LINE BROKEN.
William Lucas Admitted to Member-
ship in the Eighth Regiment.
ALAMEDA, July 26.â Among the first
young men of Alameda to respond to the
call for volunteers to fight for the coun
try was William Lucas of this place. His
father Is a veteran of the Civil War, an
inmate of the Soldiers' Home at Santa
Monica, and a member of the Grand
Army. Lucas passed two physical ex
aminations successfully, and when the
company was summoned to be mustered
Into the service he was promptly on hand.
Hut to his great disappointment he was
told by the mustering officer that as he
had a tinge of color in his complexion he
could not be permitted to enter the ser
vice of the Government. Lucas had sonu
good friends, however, and a determined
effort was made to allow him to be mus
tered In. This proved successful finally,
and on Saturday last he was sworn in
and Is now wearing the blue uniform or
a soldier.
Death of Miss Elizabeth McLean.
ALAMEDA. July 26.â MissE. M. McLean
daughter of Health Officer Dr. McLean
of this city, died last night at the family
resider.ee on Central avenue. She had
been ill but a short time from malaria!
fever, which became complicated with
cerebro spinal meningitis, until dent!
resulted. Miss McLean was an active
worker in the Salvation Army, and had
been prominent in establishing- the colony
at Soledad. Indeed it is believed that Un
seeds of disease were implanted from the
freshly turned soil there which she was
superintending. She had taken a twi
years' course at the Agricultural College
at Berkeley, and was making use of the
knowledge thus acquired to aid the colo
irists at Fort Romey. Her father is now
en route for home and is expected to ar
rive on Friday evening-. The funeral will
probably take place on Saturday

BARNETT SAID
TO BE CRAZY
Has Lived in the Tules
for a Generation.
MAY HAVE USED SOME POISON
KNOWN TO THE TOWNHIP AS
"TULE-HOG HARRY."
He Has Been Considered Not Re
sponsible for His Actions
for Many Years
Past.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, July 26.
It Is not at all unlikely that Harry
Barnett of Pleasanton may be examined
as to his sanity at the Instance of some
of his frlende. Barnett Is the man who
Is charged by his daughter with poison
ing her Infant child about a month ago.
The remains of the baby are now in the
hands of Professor Colby of the State
University for the purpose of testing the
truth of the girl's story.
Harry Barnett has long been known as
"Tule-hog Harry," and so commonly Is this
name applied to the old fellow that many
people in Murray Township did not know
that the story of young Mrs. Hoover had
any reference to him, as they hardly
ever heard him spoken of as Harry Bar
nett.
"Tule-hog Harry" Is one of the best
known characters in the township. Ho
has lived In a small house among the
tules for many years, and has always
been considered more than half crazy.
He has always been the butt of small
boys and on several occasions has re
pulsed theMr levity in such a manner as
to create small riots.
"Harry Barnett, under the name of
'Tule-hog Harry.' " said William Kelly of
Pleasanton, to-day, "has been a char
acter for years. If it should transpire
that there is poison in the baby's body
and that Its grandfather administered it,
little surprise would be expressed by
ihos>e who know old Harry. He is er
ratic, quarrelsome, and of a vicious dis
position, and this is partly due to the
continual annoyances to which he has
been subjected by the little rascals who
live near him. There is no doubt that
the old man is not responsible for his ac
tions. On one occasion he shot my broth
er's dog because he said 1J; annoyed him,
and was so careless in doing it that he
came within an ace of shooting my
brother. The old man should be exam
ined, for I do not think It is safe for
him to be at large, and In this opinion
1 am sure many others concur."
TAKING A MUCH
NEEDED VACATION
RED CROSS WORKERS HAVING A
WEEKS REST.
What the Workers In. the Society
Throughout the State Are
Doing.
An air of quiet pervades the Red Cross
supply depot at 16 Post street. The hum
of the sewing machines is stilled and the
familiar faces that have been seen there
during the past few months are missed.
All this is due to a much needed week's
vacation that the busy warkers are tak
ing.
The store is still open and Mrs. Theo
dore E. Smith and a few untiring work
ers are in attendance ready to transact
business. On Monday next the workers
will be at their posts busily engaged on
Âec-essaries for the next' expedition.
Through the courtesy of the publishers
of ihe Overland Magazine the reports
read at the open meeting of the Red
Cross Society have been run in full and
make interesting reading. A Red Cross
department will be a feature oJ the well-
Known magazine and each number will
be in turn edited by the local and State
societies. All those who were unable to
attend the famous meeting will gain a
.splendid idea of the work done by the
society by purchasing a copy of the peri
odical.
To the Publicâ Wishing to do justice in full

ciety. Said committee reported the accounts in
perfect condition, tallying with the amount

Each letter carrier of India has a run
of six miles and at the end of it Is relieve*!
by another carrier, who at once begins his
run. Thus the mall Is conveyed over un
populous sections in comparatively quick
time.
MRS. STANFORD
GETS DIVORCED
She Will Probably Marry
Friend Barmore.
CASH DOWN PAID FOR HER
iHE JOCKEY FELL IN LOVE
WITH THE SCHOOLMA'AM.
He Turns Her Over to the Man He
Befriended and Will Ride
Through Life for a
While Alone.
The marital troubles of Steeplechase
Jockey Stanford and hie wife, Gertrude,
are over. A compromise has been ef
fected whereby the co-respondent In the
Australian's divorce suit, Barmore, by
the payment of a certain goodly sum,
takes Mrs. Stanford off the hands of her
husband for good and all.
That Stanford is pleased does not ex
press It. and that Barmore will marry
the erring woman who Is now allowed
a divorce Is fairly certain. The story
back of the trouble of the Stanfords la
a rather remarkable one.
Rider Stanford married Gertrude back
In Chicago something over three years
ago, when under contract to Messrs. Cor
rigan and Brenock. Being Impressed
with the fact that she was an educated
woman, he grew to have affec
tion for her and married the woman,
bringing her to California. Here he told
his friends that she was a schoolma'am.
She had been one In a little town in Wis
consin some years previously, so that no
person out this way suspected that the
meek little woman had been other than
what he represented.
Some time after the Stanfords had come
to the Golden State they met Barmore,
who was connected with a wholesale
jewelry establishment. Barmore went to
the races, and, according to the steeple
chase rider, lost considerable money, fin
ally becoming "short" $200 in his ac
counts. Stanford influenced him to bet
$200 upon Gold Dust (his mount) one day,
and as the horse won at odds of 8 to 1,
the young jeweler was enabled to make
good his shortage and to have a neat
sum with which to continue his turf
operations. Naturally Stanford thought
Barmore would ever be his friend.
Early last spring the horse pilot was
suspended indefinitely by the Pacific
Coast Jockey Club, and not seeing any
signs of being reinstated, he went to
India, where he had formerly ridden race
horses. While in Singapore he received
a letter from an old friend living at
Benlcia, Cal., which led him to believe
all was not well within his household.
I- urthermore, it was hinted in the epistle
that his wife and others had plotted to
get him out of the country.
Stanford immediately took the steamer
for San Francisco, coming by way of
Shanghai. One of the first persons he
met here was Barmore, who informed
him that Mrs. Stanford had gone to Chi
cago. Barmore kindly offered to let Stan
ford have the money to take nim to the
Middle West metropolis and urged him
to go at once. In a day or so,
when rested up. he left. On his arrival
at Chicago he met Hand, the handicap
per, who had known the couple well In
California. Hand not only informed
Stanford that his erring wife was not
there but that he suspected she had sold
all the household effects and was living
with Barmore as Mrs. Barmore.
Upon his return here the timber-top
ping jockey found that Hand's suspicions
were very correct. While Stanford was
in the Windy City his wife had brought
suit for divorce on the grounds of cruelty
and desertion, and he returned in the nick
of time to defend himself. So Infuriated
was Stanford over what he had learned
that upon meeting Barmore he "took a
shot" at the despoiler of his home, the
bullet doing no further damage than to
lacerate the jewelry man's coai tails.
Barmore, for some reason best known to
himself, merely had Stanford arrested
and charged with threatening his life,
saying nothing of the shot the rider had
taken at him.
Stanford pot out of the Barmore trouble
and his lawyer wanted to have the Jew
elry man arrested.
Then followed the compromise by which
Stanford is enriched considerably and
Barmore is now entitled to wed Gertrude,
the Wisconsin schoolteacher, etc.

SAILS HOPEFUL
AND IN HEALTH
Liliuokalani Leaves for
the Islands.
HAS NO TRACE OF CANCER
CHANGE OF CLIMATE CAUSES
DEPRESSION.
The Ex-Queen of Hawaii Has Politi
cal and Financial Worries,
but No Organic
Disease.
Hawaii's ex-Queen, Lllluokalanl, sail
eel yesterday to revisit her late king
dom. She loft in the best of health and
sjiirlts. Her companions, after the
pretty Hawaiian custom, showered
flowers upon her to brighten her de
parture. TherÂ« was not a trace of
tears, for was not the Queen going back
to hÂ«=r <>wn people, even though they
were no longer her subjects? She knew
that faithful hands would greet her,
that faithful hearts would sustain her
through the many trials which beset
her rath.
The statement which appeared in an
evening paper yesterday that the ex-
Queen was suffering from cancer on
the right side of the neck is absolutely
untrue. She has no trace of any such
disease. The worst ailment with which
she has been afflicted is an occasional
attack of nervous depression.
"I can easily understand how such a
misapprehension arose," said Colonel
Macfarlane last night. "Her Majesty
has suffered a good deal from the
change of climate during the past two
years, during most of which time she
has resided in Washington. When com
ing to this coast, feeling nervous and
out of sorts, she thought it better that
Dr. English, a well known Eastern
practitioner, should accompany her.
"Dr. English, after arriving here,
thought he would like to try his luck
as a medical practitioner in Honolulu,
so he obtained her Majesty's permission
to accompany her to the islands. Now
thÂ« doctor has achieved fame in the
East as a cancer specialist, and there
you have the explanation of the story."
Colonel Macfarlane is remaining be
hind in San Francisco to watch the
ex-Queen's interests. He admits that,
in addition to climatic influences, polit
ical and financial worries may have
had a good deal to do with her fits of
nervous depression. "The Queen," he
says, "has mortgaged nearly all her
private property in order to meet the
expenses of her two years' residence in
Washington. She has refused all of
fers of aid from private friends, being
confident that her own resources Would
suffice to bring her through.
"She had almost attained success, the
annexation scheme was on the point of
defeat, when the war name, altering
the whole situation. Until then she
had kept up well through all trials, but
the shock was too great. Her nervous
system gave way for the time, and
hence the necessity for a medical at
tendant.
'"The Queen is not going to Hawaii
with any aggressive motive. She is
quite content to accept the Inevitable
nr.d will not offer any active opposition
t<-> annexation. Of course she expects
to receive Just treatment from the
T'niteii States and to obtain a fair com
pensation for the crown lands, which
have been taken from her by the re
public of Hawaii. But she wi'i not id
vise her subjects to resist and unless
th<"'y call upon her, by an overwhelming
vote, to oppose annexation, she will do
nothing to prevent the union of Hawaii
with the I'nited Stare*. She realizes
that her cause is lost, but her beloved
People are still left, to her and their
will is hers."

NO MECHANICS'
FAIR IN 1898
This Year's Exposition
Has Been Abandoned.
TOO MANY FAIRS THE CAUSE
NATIVE DAUGHTERS ROAST
INSTITUTE TRUSTEES.
They Say They Were Treated Like
Prize-Fight Promoters and Tell
How â Some Caustic State
ments.
There was a rumor current on the
street yesterday that the trustees of the
Mechanics' Institute were greatly wor
ried over a shortage or deficit of several
thousand dollars in the receipts for the
last fiscal year. According to the state
ment of Rodney Kendrlck, vice-president
of the institution, however, the shortage
hns not materialized, hut there has been
much anxiety as to whether the institute
would be able to hold its own during the
balance of the calendar year.
Some time ago financial conditions be
came so unsatisfactory that it was found
necessary to appoint a committee to In
vestigate the situation and report the re
sults with suggestions as to means to be
adopted to turn the adverse tide into one
of prosperity. The results of the labors
of this committee have not been made
public, but one particular point they em
phasized was the fact that if the loss of
membership In the library continued aa
it had been maintained In the preceding
three months the institute would find it
self at the end of the current year with
a very awkward deficit to provide for.
The knowledge of these bad conditions
led to a general discussion of affairs
among the trustees regarding the policy
to be pursued under these circumstances.
It had been Intended to hoid the Me
chanics' Fair as usual in the hope of add
ing to the general fund, but after long
and earnest consideration it was tacit
ly agreed that it would not be wise to
give an exposition this year lest it prove
a losing instead of a paying proposition,
ar.d the announcement win probably be
made at the next regular meeting ot the
âmere Is no deficit at the present
time." said Yice-Pfesident Kendrick yes
terday afternoon. In speaking ot the
rumor regarding the institute, "but we
feared at one time that (or several rea
sons particularly on account of the heavy
loss of membership that came with the
prospect of war, that we wonld surfer a
loss. Lately, however, we flnd that the
roll of members is increasing Bteadlly, and
if that keeps up we will be all right. We
hud intended to hold a fnir with special
features this year, but after looking over
the situation "we concluded it was not
promising.
"Thert- have been the Miners' Fair, the
Masonic Fair, the several military exhi
bitions, and now cotnes the Irish Fair.
It was not reasonable to suppose that the
merchants and manufacturers, on whom
. irgely depend for the success of our
exposition!", would care to do more of that
kind Of advertising this season, and a.s
a conseo.uer.ee we have decided that we
would not be displaying good business
sense to hold a fair, bo there will be
none this year at least. Between now and
the time to hold the exposition in Kk>
\\n will endeavor to devise some new
and taking features which will assure its
success.
"Of course the Institute is quite
wealthy, tor instance, our pavilion prop
erty alone is worth anywhere from $750,uu0
to Jl,u<'w,ikiO. Then we have the. Mission
blocks, which are very valuable. How
ever, that property is not all paid for, as
will be seen by the fact thai we arc now
paying $c>Ot per month interest on the in
debtedness. This drain, jrittt other regu
lar expenses, has to lie met, and you
know we always want to show a good
substantial net proilt at the end of each
year. 1 think things will run along nicely
from this time on.'
'ihe Native Daughters, Red Cross Aid,
which gave the highly successful drill of
tne Seventh Regiment at the Pavilion last
Thursday night, has something to say
with reference to what It calls "The
penuriousiiess of this wealthy public in
stitution." The entertainment was given
for the purpose Of raising money to send
to Colonel Smith for the benefit of the
First Regiment. California Volunteers, at
Manila, and the public responded to the
worthy call in a most gratifying manner.
"We innocently hoped when we under
took the work," said one of the officers
of the Native Daughters' organization,
"that It 3 charitable object would receive
some consideration from the Mechanics'
Institute. The Seventh Regiment pre
pared for and did its arduous work gladly,
and the Native Sons' band volunteered to
furnish concert and dance music, but
when we approached the trustees of the
Institute we found that we had sterner
stuff to deal with. We naturally supposed
that an institution that counted its wealth
by tiie hundred.-- 02 thousands of dollars
and which derived its property and sup
port from the public would In these jimes
of war and its horrors display charity
and patriotism ,is the thousands of citi
zens of tiiis city nnd State have been
and are now doing. When we asked for
terms on the use of the- Pavilion we were
curtly refused, ami to our surprise found
that we were, so far as rental was con
cerned, classified with the promoters of
prize lights and every other private en
terprise. We appealed to their patriotism,
and asked for a reduction, but could not
get one dollar concession. They charged
us .tlijp for the use of the binding, and
saiti the gas would cost $J'i. When we
got the bill we found that we had not
only been charged $14 for gas. but *2 for
turning It on and %'i for the fact that the
fixtures were equipped with a 'patent
burner.' The public, we believe, will
agree with us tn.it this was a display ol
a miserly s;.irit and what makes It worst
is the circumstance that it comes from
tho managers of this great popular pub
lic institution. '
"I must not forget to mention the
smallest and meanest thing done by the
trusu-ep. After charring us the top tlg
ure w<' were Informed that we must send
two free tickets to each trustee. Think
of that, and they were twenty-five cent
tickets too. Compare this with the fact
that every one of the women who organ
izi<l the entertainment paid for admis
sion, and the daily press gave us columns
of their valuable space to assist in mak
ing this charity work a success.
"However, without any thanks to the
trustees of tin- Mechanics' Library, and
with heartfelt thanks to the thousands
of citizens and soldier boys who cheer
fully gave their aid, the Native Daugh
ters are proud and happy to say they
cleared nearly $Wj at that drill. ' Every
cent of this money will be cabled to
Honrkorst and fmm there forwarded
to the First Regiment boys to provide
for them In sickness and hardship."

A Runaway Accident.
SAN RAFAEL, July 26.â What almost
resulted' in the the death of Mrs.
Patrick Connolly, wife of a San Quen
tln guard, in a runaway accident, oc
curred yesterday afternoon. Connolly and
his wife drove over from the prison, and,
stopping in front of Sutherland's har
ness shop, Connolly took the bridle off
his horse to have it mended. Taking
fright at a passing bicyclist, the horse
dashed down Fourth street. Near the
broad gauge depot the buggy was over
turned ana wrecked and Mrs. Connolly
was throv.-n out with such force as to
render her unconscious. An examination
showed that her arm was broken and
that she was severely bruised and cut on
the face and hands.
Native Sons' Installation.
SAN RAFAEL, July 2Câ The annual In
stallation of the officers of iiiount Tamal
pois Parlor No. 64, Native Sons of the
Golden West, took place last evening.
District Deputy J. Niff officiated. A ban
quet was tendered the officers by the
members. They are: Worthy president,
Fred Harfer; first worthy vice president,
M. F. Cochrane; second vice, George
Herzog; third vice, Emmett Hay den;
worthy marshal, Steven Richardson;
worthy inside guard, J. H. Watson Jr.
Arnett Taken Captive.
SUSANVTLI,E. July 2>s.â Arnett, who
shot his wife yesterday, was captured
to-day about five miles west of Hayden
Hill. 'The Sheriff is bringing him to Su
sanville. His wife still is suffering from
her wounds. The motive Tor the shooting
is not known.
Mourning in Woodland.
WOODLAND, July 26.â Miss Minnie
Lee, Bister of Miss Harriet Lee. president
of the County Board of Education, died,
after a long and painful illness, at about
11 o'clock this morning.

MAGUIRE MAY
SOON HAVE TO
TURN HIS COAT
His Position in the Democratic
and Populist Parties Still
in Doubt.
The Statesman's Opinion of His Own Party
as Set Forth Over His Own
Signature.
Since the pinto Populist convention
nominated James G. Maguire for Gov
ernor a change seems to have come
over the spirit of the Democratic po
litical dream. The good Democrats
who have all along professed to see in
the "little giant" the man o^ the hour
are beginning to suspect that they have
been handed a gold brick by the king
of all political bunko sharps.
The Democrats who have not looked
unkindly on the proposition to fuse
â with the Populists flnd that the Con
gressman has so manipulated things
that the only way in which fusion can
te brought about is by the placing of
Maguire's name at the head of the
Democratic State ticket. Maguire, be
sides being a statesman and a politi
cian, is a lawyer, and among lawyers
such tricks are classed under the gen
eral head of "'sharp practice." Sharp
practice tricks are not in high favor
with honest men, and that is why
honest Democrats are scratching their
heads and thinking instead of wearing
overalls and letting their beards grow
in honor of the action taken by the
tenderloin portion of the late lamented
Populist convention.
Now that the men who form the bet
ter element of the Democratic party
have begun to speculate as to the de
gree of their servitude they are mani
festing a disposition to inquire more
closely into the character of the man
who called the men of the American
navy pirates, and who, not so very
long ago foreswore allegiance to the
party which he now asks to join with
the Populists in honoring him with the
nomination for Governor of the State of
California. Not only did Maguire
withdraw from the party, but he called
it bad names, just as he did the men
who, with Sampson and Schley and
Dewey, have been fighting for the
honor and glory of the nation. He said
that the Democratic party held wealth
as the standard of respectability; that
it was a bidder for the affection of
monopolists, and that it ignored the
masses. He said much more to the
same effect and then proceeded to rub
it in by accusing the party in the fol
lowing language:
"The entire solid area of our great
country, including the natural oppor
tunities of all our peopleâ the natural
resources without which none of our
c can liveâ has been made the ex
clusive property of a few thousand
alien and domestic landlords, who by
â \ irtue of the absolute control which
they do thus, as owners, exercise over
the only means of living of our land
less millions, are robbing the latter
continuously of from one-half to four
fifths of the fruits of their labor, and
are daily driving them to lower and
lower depths of slavery and hapless
misery. This !s a system of servitude
just as complete as chattel slavery and
in many respects more atrocious; yet
the so-called Democratic party, while
claiming to be the friend of the la
borer, expressly defends this system
and bids for the support of those who
profit by it. As a self-respecting man
I feel justified and bound in honor to
â withdraw from a party which haa
fallen to such levels."
That is what the statesman once
thought of the party he now asks to
give him the highest office in the State.
As the party has not changed since the
Congressman penned those bitter
â words It is to be presumed that he has
experienced a change of heart consist
ent with his ever growing desire to
keep his hand in the public purse.
"THE GHOST"
SHOT MILLS
Solution of a Murder
Mystery.
IMPORTANT CLEWDISCOVERED
POLICE LOOKING FOR AN EX
CONVICT NAMED SULLIVAN.
Perpetrator of a Crime Committed
Three Years Ago â Suspect As
sisted McNulty to
Escape.
The police believe that If they can lo
cate "Ghost" Sullivan, a notorious
crook and ex-convict, they will have
cleared up the mystery surrounding the
tragic death of Richard Nevills, an un
fortunate carpenter who was shot and
killed by a masked robber about three
years ago.
Nevills on the night that he was
murdered visited a saloon kept by a
man named Hlldebrand at Greenwich
and Pierce streets, intending to have a
drink. A few minutes after he had
entered the saloon two masked robbers
appeared, and, covering Nevills and
Hlldebrand with their revolvers, or
dered them to throw up their hands.
Nevills lost no time in complying with
the request, evidently realizing that he
was in the hands of two desperate men.
Hildebrand, howc^ver, seeing a chance
to escape, crawled under the bar and
got into a rear room. The next instant
a shot rang out, and Nevills fell, mor
tally wounded. The desperate robbers
at once left the saloon, without having
attempted to rob the place.
A short time after they had disap
peared Hildebrand mustered up suf
ficient courage to emerge from his hid
ing place, and, going to a saloon about
a block distant, he notified the proprie
tor of the killing of Nevills. The police
were then notified, and they proceeded
to make an investigation. Several sua
pects were arrested, but as they suc
There are those who are not unwill
ing to believe that Maguire's former
demonstration of his ability to change
his political coat at a moment's notice
will stand him in good stead in the
emergency that now confronts him. He
has received the Populist nomination
for Governor and to accept it he will
have to become a Populist. In nomi
nating him the Populists attached a
string to their act by imposing as a
condition the proviso that the nomina
tion would not be valid unless Ma
guire accepted the Populist plat'orm
within fifteen days after his arrival
within the borders of the State. But
such n condition is no obstacle to a
statesman of Maguire's ability. He is
still in Washington, and even were he
to start for California immediately he
would not arrive here until after the
first day of August. The Democratic
convention will probably be held on
August 16, or before the fifteen-day
Populist time limit would expire.
Should he be so fortunate as to secure
the Democratic nomination for Gover
nor he could then accept the Populist
nomination, condition and all, with
thanks, and the Populists could inaug
urate a guessing contest on the ques
tion of his sincerity. If he does not
receive the Democratic nomination he
would then be In a position to reject
the Populist proposition with scorn and
to reiterate his adherence to the Dem
ocratic principles which he once upon
a time so gayly Spat upon. To the
man up a tree it would appear that the
wily statesman has his beloved Demo
cratic party on the hip and the piebald
Populists in chancery-
Maguire's expressed belief that the
men of the American navy are no bet
ter than pirates has apparently been
followed by a public display of affec
tion for the Spaniards. An evening
paper which has all along evinced a
fondness for the statesman publishes a
Washington telegram under the follow
ing scare head:
MAGUIRE WILL
NOT TALK MUCH
Says That the Condition of Coast
Politics Will Not Per
mit It.
He Had Great Difficulty in Avoiding
the Crowds That Followed
Him About.
Is Fond of Cervera and Desires to Visit
and Thank the Admiral for His
Kindness to Him.
It would appear from this that Ma
guire has a special liking for the Span
ish admiral and has received favors
at his hands. In the light of this
publication it is even possible of belief
that some secret understanding existed
between the statesman and the Span
ish admiral at the very time when the
former called the operations of our
navy acts of piracy. The matter
might well be made the subject of a
searching inquiry were it not for the
fact that the paper which published
the story is noted for inaccuracy, and
the additional fact that on another
page of the same paper there appeared
a telegraphic item concerning the ar
rival in Washington of Lieutenant
Hobson. It is barely possible that the
latter part of the scare head referred to
Hobson and not to Magulre.
ceeded in satisfying the police of their
innocence they were released. A short
time after the commission of the crime
two detectives who were detailed on the
case heard of a conversation which
clearly proves that Sullivan, alias "the
Ghost," was one of the masked robbers.
After leaving the scene of the murder
the robbers headed for the water front,
and realizing that they were safe from
arrest entered a saloon kept by a man
named Kerwin. Sullivan seemed dis
appointed at not "getting the stuff," as
he expressed It, and, turning to his
companion, accused him of being a
"dub."
"If you had held up that Dutch
man," he is said to have remarked, "I
would not have been forced to have shot
the other guy."
His companion replied that he did
not want to kill him, which seemed to
anger Sullivan.
"You wanted me to kill the other fel
low, though," he angrily retorted.
"If you had been on to your Job you
would have stored the boss from en
tering the rear room, and we could
have copped off the dough."
Trembling with â¢ fear his companion
in crime declared that he was glad
that he had not shot Hildebrand.
"You didn't have to kill him," re
marked his murderous accomplice. "If
you had placed your gun under his nose
he would have quit. Instead of making
the play good you let him get away. I
had to stand for the other guy (mean
ing Nevills) and when he kicked I
pumped it into him. I hope he is dead,
as he had no right to interfere, as we
were not after his stuff."
After having several rounds of drinks
the men left the saloon, evidently un
mindful of the fact that their conver
sation had been overheard. A hanger
on who had listened to their conversa
tion informed the police, and several
detectives were sent to find Sullivan
and his accomplice. Despite their ef
forts the men escaped, presumably
having taken passage on a Bteamer
bound for the north.
Several days ago word was received
at police headquarters stating that Sul
livan was In Seattle. A dispatch ask
ing for his arrest was sent to the Chief
of Police of that city, but no answer
has been received as yet.
Sullivan is a desperate character and
the police are satisfied that he will not
be taken alive. Some years ago Sulli
van, who is a physical wreck, from the
excessive use of opium, was arrested
for burglary and committed to the
County Jail. He became acquainted
with John McNultv who was under
sentence of death for the murder of a
man named Collins, whom he killed In
cold blood. Evidently thinking that he
would be hanged, McNulty took Sulli
van Into his confidence and together
they planned a way to escape. They
were assisted by several other convicts,
who were In the Jail at the time. The
desperate men bored their way to the
outer wall of the jail, and during an
THE SAN FKANCTSCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, JULY 27, 1898.
HIS FLOCK WILL MOURN HIM.
REV. M. M. GIBSON, D, D., Pastor of the First United
Presbyterian Church, Who Died Yesterdau While
on a Vacation in Oregon.
Rev.. M. M. Gibson, D.D.. pastor of the First United Presbyterian
Church of this city, died very suddenly yesterday morning at 4 o'clock at
the residence of his daughter, Mrs. Percy Young, at Albany, Or. The sad
Information came In a dispatch received yesterday by Assistant District
Attorney A. P. Black, who is president of the board of trustees of the
church.
No details of Dr. Gibson's illness were given in the telegram, but It has
been known to his friends here for a long time past that he has been suf
fering from throat trouble, and also had a slight affection of the heart. The
remains will be brought here for interment, and will arrive by the Oregon
express at 8:15 o'clock to-morrow morning.
On April 3 last Dr. Gibson and wife left here on their annual three
months' vacation to visit relatives in the East. Leaving here he went to
Pittsbur- to visit his wife's parents. The weather was exceedingly bad at
the time, and he wrote to friends here that he was suffering from a severe
cold, which had aggravated his throat trouble. From Pittsburg Dr. and
Mrs. Gibson went to Chicago to visit John Marshall Jr.. an insurance man
well known in San Francisco. Then they journeyed to Monroe, lowa, to see
Dr. Gibson's brother, the Rev. J. B. Gibson, and to Monmouth, 111., to his
brother-in-law. Pr. Hrown.
From there they c^ parted for Albany, Or., to spend the balance of their
vacation with their only surviving child, Mrs. Percy Young, who as Florence
Gibson was very well known in the foremost church and social circles of
this city.
Dr. Gibson's health did not return, as he had hoped for after arriving
in Oregon, and he wrote to the trustees of the church and was granted an
additional month's leave of absence. He kept up his correspondence with
many friends here, and none had any idea that his illness was anything
more than temporary and hoped for his speedy recovery. Its fatal termin
ation will be a sad blow to his flock, who had looked forward with pleasur
able antlcinations to his early return to the pulpit of their church.
Matthew McCormick Gibson was born in Central Pennsylvania about 63
years ago. Ho attended various ecclesiastical colleges at Allegheny City
and other places in Pennsylvania, and allied himself with the doctrines of
the United Presbyterians. Early In lSt><> a Dr. Cooper came to San Francisco
and established the First United Presbyterian Church of this city, holding
its meetings in Swan's Hall, on the south side of Market street, about
opposite O'Farrell. After remaining here about four months and seeing
the church well established Dr. Cooper resigned the pastorate and recom
mended to his followers a young man named Gibson, then at Pittsburg. Dr.
Gibson was called and accepted, arriving here on a Sunday morning In Oc
tober, 18C6, and held his charge up to the day of his death, nearly thirty
two years.
The church thrived under Dr. Gibson's management, and soon moved to
larger quarters at the City College, Stockton and Geary streets, where the
Spring Valley Water Works building now stands, in 1870 the church erected
an edifice of its own on the west side of Mason street, near Eddy, which
was sold in 1593, when they moved to the present commodious edifice at
the corner of Golden Gate avenue and Polk street.
Dr. Gibson was loved by all who knew him for his kindly disposition
and beneficent temperament. Over six feet in height, with a kindly face
and Mowing silvery beard, his appearance marked his calling, and his sunny
nature never turned a deaf ear to a deserving charity.
The loss of his son nearly twenty years ago was a terrible blow to him,
but he bore up under it with true fortitude. He leaves surviving him a
widow and one daughter, residing in Oregon.
Dr and Mrs. Gibson resided at the manse, 1518 Fulton street, built by
the church in 1894. It was Intended to make this the initiative some time
In the future in the forming of a new church organization.

Excursion to Santa Cruz.
The excursion to Santa Cruz next Sun
day will be personally conducted by Wil
liam H. Menton, the excursion passenger
agent of the Southern Pacific Company,
and promises to be one of the most pleas
urable outings of the summer. The trip
will give the excursionists five hours at
the seaside and the price is but $2. Among
the attractions booked for the day are a
?rand concert on the beach and an old
ashioned mussel bake, which will begin
at 2 o'clock In the afternoon. Persons cc
siring to take advantage of the excursion
should take the narrow-gauge boat from
San Franoiso at 7:45 a. m. Oakland people
should take the 7:45 train at Fourteenth
and Franklin streets, and those from Ala
meda should take the 8:20 a. m. train
from Park street. The returning train
will reach San Francisco at 8:05 in the
evening.
"JIM" REA PROMPTLY
CALLED TO ACCOUNT
Major Frank McLaughlin Satiates
the Former Boss' Desire for
Satisfaction.
James W. Rea, bully and former
boss of San Jose, smarting over his
downfall, came up from San Jose yes
terday morning "full panoplied for
war.' Since his ejectment from the
Republh an party by the State Central
Committee last Saturday Rea has been
furious, and on the train yesterday his
loud-mouthed boastings of what he tn
tended to do to those who had acoom
phehfed Ms exit from the political arena
disgusted all the other passenger.
With the proverbial chip carefully
balanced on his shoulder, Rea made h's
way up town looking for trouble, and
found it before he had hardly begun
his quest.
Almoft the first man he met was Ma
jor Frank McLaughlin, the author cf
fill hi.s troubles, and on him Rea opened
his batteries. The return flre came so
promptly and vigorously that the bully
was Instantly cowed. Quickly assum
ing a conciliatory air Rea forced a grin
on his features and lamely at
tempted to pass his words off as a Joke.
Major McLaughlin, however, could not
see the humor of the affair, and gave
Rea to understand that such Jokes did
not go with him. The late boss be
lieved him, for he removed the chip
from his shoulder then and there and
sought no further for the scalps of his
executioners.

and closets had been ransacked and sev
eral neat bundles of Jewelry and silver
ware showed that burglars had been on
the point of decamping with their plun
der. The police are investigating the mat
ter.

WON COLD
CASH BY A
COMPROMISE
Famous Foley Case Is
Concluded.
SEQUEL TO THE SHOOTING
THE NEVADA SENATOR'S ES
TATE BATED AT $100,000.
Eastern Heirs Consider That They
Gained Materially by Settling
the Complicated Case Out
of Court.
George W. Baker returned yesterday
from Reno, Nev., where he has been
engaged for a week or more in the con
test of the petition for the distribution
of the late Senator Foley's estate. By
a compromise which was made at the
eleventh hour Mrs. Oscar J. Smith, the
Senator's widow, paid the Eastern
heirs, whom Mr. Baker and Judge
Blgelow represented, $25,000 in cash.
Mr. Baker considers this even better
than the winning of his suit, for if he
had secured a decision giving them all
the interest in the estate they asked it
would never have realized this amount.
In speaking- of the case last evening,
Mr. Baker said: "This was a very com
plicated and peculiar case. Senator
Foley was shot and killed by a Mrs.
Hartley in 1894. She was tried, con
victed and sent to prison for ten years.
After serving two years, she was par
doned last year by Governor Sadler.
"The Foley estate was appraised at
over $100,000, but shrank in value with
everything else in Nevada. He left no
will, and under the State law the widow
secures afl of the community property
and half of all the rest. Mr. Foley's
mother, three sisters and brother in the
East employed us to look after their in
terests. The widow signed an agree
ment to divide the property equally,
taking half and giving the Eastern rel
atives half.
"Just at this time Mrs. Hartley was
pardoned and came upon the Bcene
with a son, whom she claimed was Fo
ley's offspring. She filed a suit for
half of the property for this boy. Just
before the case went to trial the boy
died. She had her father appointed
administrator of the child's estate, con
sisting of this claim against Foley's
estate. The suit was tried last sum
mer, and as his claim would prevent
the Eastern relatives from securing
anything if he won, we fought and de
feated that case. No appeal was ta
ken, and in this way Mrs. Hartley
dropped out of the case entirely, al
though she watched it constantly, at
tending every session.
"Mrs. Foley, who had remarried,
then repudiated the agreement to di
vide the property equally, claiming
that her attorney had misinformed her
and not given her the proper advice.
She secured the services of Messrs.
Campbell, Clark and Rand. The case
was tried in Washoe County before a
jury last fall and we secured a ver
dict for a half of the estate. Mrs.
Foley's attorney, who drew up the con
tract for an equal distribution of the
estate, was on the stand at that time
and in every way possible tried to
make it appear that the contract did
not mean what it said, but to no avail.
The case was appealed on some point
of instructions, reversed and sent
down for another triai.
"This began on July 18 and continued
all last week- We had a good case,
but it never reached the Jury for a ver
dict. Before the close the attorneys
for Mrs. Smith advised a compromise.
This was decided upon and Mrs. Smith
paid us $25,000 for the Eastern heirs,
which is more than we could have re
alized If the Jury had given us all we
apked, for I do not believe that the en
tire estate could be sold to-day for 550,
000. The settlement took place on Sat
urday last."
ENTERTAIN MANY
CALIFORNIA BOYS
Sixth and Seventh Regiments Guests
of California Street M. E. Church
Society.
Nearly 1000 members of the Sixth and
Seventh Regiments, California Volun
teers, were the guests last night of the
Epworth League of California-street
Methodist Episcopal Church.
An excellent musical and literary pro
gramme was rendered in the auditorium
of the church by soldier boys and by
members of the church under direction
of EL B. Paamore.
The Seventh Regiment band rendered
the "Star Spangled Banner," the audi
ence standing. The band then struck up
a lively march, during the rendition of
which four little girls: Edith Brant Car
rie McLane, Violet Laflln and Maggie
Kenny, went among the guests distribut
ing bouquets.
The address of welcome was delivered
by J. H. Northon, president of the Ep
worth League of the church. The quartet
from the Santa Paula Company, E, con
tributed several vocal numbers. Misa
Gardner sang sweetly, and M. F. Pears,
solo clarinetist of the band, astonished
his auditors with several whistling solos.
Mles Mabel Brown recited "Elder
Lamb's Donation," Mrs. Bertha Eckman
sang "Remember the Maine," and Mr.
Spencer, of Company D, sang a ballad.
At the conolusion of the programme re
freshments were served by the ladles of
the church in the assembly room.
Polk- Street Merchants Jubilant.
The fact that the Supervisors have rec
ognized the necessity of illuminating the
business streets of the city by an extra
supply of electric lights has pleased the
Polk street merchants, and they are con
gratulating one another on their fore
sight in being the originators of the
swinging lamps in the center of the
streets for the lighting of that thorough
fare.
By the called-for contract for lights for
the city for the next two âears all the
prominent business streets will oe sup
plied with extra electric lights until mid
night.
Having succeeded so well in this matter
the Polk street merchants will gather to
gether in the near future and reorganize
on a more substantial basis. The first and
most important movement /ill be to get
the Sutter Street Railroad Company to
join with the Union street line and extend
its route along Polk street to the waters
of the bay, the company having already a
franchise for that part of Polk street
through the hogsback, which Is now cut
to the street grade.
As soon as this cut gets paved and ac
cepted the privilege of running teams on
Van Ness avenue, which has been de
clared a boulevard, will be withdrawn
and all traffic will be directed to Polk
street.
Since the Bush street boulevard "scare"
has passed by the people residing on this
street would gratefully contribute for the
purchase of a leather medal for the pres
ident of the Sutter Street Railroad Com
pany if he would instruct the superin
tendent to fill in the furrows left, in the
middle of the street by the removal of
the rails over which the ghost car used
to materialize once a day.
